تخلخل
تكاثف
خلاء
دور
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mutakallimun completely reject rarefaction and condensation as impossible.
Correction: In this passage, they argue that rarefaction and condensation can account for motion in a plenum without void or collision, and it is the philosophers who must refute them.

Checkpoint
According to the mutakallimun, how can motion in a plenum avoid void and collision without needing rarefaction or condensation?
A) By the body moving at infinite speed.
B) By God annihilating the body in front and creating a new body behind.
C) By the air in front pushing back the mover.
D) By the bodies being infinitely divisible.

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun's response to the second ilzam argument refocuses the debate on whether rarefaction and condensation are possible, shifting the burden to the philosophers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows two different accounts of how motion happens: divine intervention or natural processes like rarefaction and condensation. When you experience an unexpected change in your life (like a sudden illness or a new opportunity), do you instinctively attribute it to Allah's direct control or to natural causes? How does your answer reveal what you truly believe about God's role in every moment? This week, try to consciously thank Allah for even the smallest movements in your day, reminding yourself that nothing occurs without His will.
?
The philosophers' argument led to an infinite regress, which the mutakallimun avoided by appealing to Allah's power. Is there an area of your life where you feel trapped in an endless cycle (like habitual sins) that you keep trying to fix on your own? Are you relying on your own 'natural' efforts instead of turning to Allah to 'annihilate' the obstacle and 'create' a new path? Commit today to a specific du'a or action that seeks divine intervention to break that cycle.

Objection and Answer: Rarefaction and Condensation Do Not Require Void
An objection that rarefaction and condensation require void, and the answer appealing to hyle's capacity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the mutakallimun respond to the objection that rarefaction and condensation necessitate the existence of void by appealing to the nature of hyle (hayula).

Core Idea
Rarefaction and condensation are explained by hyle's receptivity, not by void.

The existence of void (khala') was a contentious issue between the mutakallimun and the philosophers. Here, an objector argues that observable phenomena like rarefaction and condensation can only be explained by void spaces between parts. The answer rejects this premise and offers an alternative explanation rooted in the nature of hyle (hayula), the substrate of bodies. Let's examine this argument closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
First, read the full Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall argument. Notice the structure: a conditional objection followed by a denial and alternative explanation.
2
Understand the objector's claim
Identify why the objector thinks rarefaction and condensation imply void: changes in volume are attributed to changes in void spaces between parts.
3
Analyze the answer's logic
The answer denies the premise and asserts that rarefaction and condensation are due to hyle's capacity to receive different magnitudes. Consider what this means: hyle has no inherent magnitude, so it can 'put on' a larger or smaller magnitude.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: Why is this answer important? It allows the mutakallimun to explain physical phenomena without accepting void, which has theological implications regarding God's power and the nature of bodies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : التخلخل والتكاثف في الأجسام إنما يكونان لكثرة الخلاء وقلته فيما بين أجزاء الجسم فيكون مقداره مع كثرة الخلاء فيما بينهما كبيراً، ومع قلته صغيراً فهما يستلزمان وقوع الخلاء الذي هو المطلوب قلنا: ممنوع كونهما لما ذكرتم بل هما لأن الهيولى أمر قابل للمقدار الصغير والكبير إذ لا مقدار لها في حد نفسها ونسبتها إلى المقادير الصغيرة والكبيرة على سواء فتخلع مقداراً وتلبس مقداراً آخر أصغر أو أكبر وسيأتي ذلك فيما بعد
For it is said: Rarefaction and condensation in bodies only occur due to the abundance and scarcity of void between the parts of the body, so its magnitude becomes large with much void between them, and small with little void. Thus they necessitate the occurrence of void, which is what is sought. We say: It is denied that they are as you mentioned. Rather, they are because hyle (hayula) is a thing receptive to small and large magnitude, as it has no magnitude in itself, and its relation to small and large magnitudes is equal. So it sheds one magnitude and puts on another, smaller or larger. And this will come later.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The objector argues that rarefaction and condensation (takhalkhul and takathuf) can only be explained by the increase or decrease of void spaces between the parts of a body. Thus, if a body rarely, its volume increases because there is more void between its parts; if it condenses, its volume decreases because there is less void. Therefore, rarefaction and condensation imply the existence of void (khala'), which is exactly what the mutakallimun were trying to avoid. The answer rejects this premise: rarefaction and condensation are not due to void but to the nature of hyle (hayula), which is a substrate that has no inherent magnitude and is equally receptive to any magnitude. When a body rarely, the hyle 'sheds' (takhla') its current magnitude and 'puts on' (talbas) a larger one; when it condenses, it puts on a smaller one. No void is needed. The author notes that this topic will be discussed later.

Key Terms
تخلخل
تكاثف
خلاء
هيولى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mutakallimun deny the reality of rarefaction and condensation.
Correction: They affirm rarefaction and condensation but explain them without void, by appealing to hyle's capacity to change magnitude.

Checkpoint
According to the answer given, what explains rarefaction and condensation instead of void?
A) The change in the number of atoms.
B) The capacity of hyle to receive different magnitudes.
C) The elasticity of the air.
D) The force of motion.

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun reject void but explain rarefaction and condensation through hyle's capacity to change magnitude, preserving a non-void model of bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When your faith feels like it has 'rarefied' (become weak) or 'condensed' (become strong), do you blame external voids (circumstances, people, lack of opportunities) or do you recognize that your heart, like hyle, is equally receptive to any magnitude by Allah's will? Will you today abandon excuses and ask Him to clothe your soul with the size He chooses?
?
The objector assumed void was necessary for change. What 'voids' (missing resources, knowledge, or conditions) do you assume are necessary for your spiritual growth that are actually not needed? Are you willing to shed that assumption and put on the belief that Allah can change you directly, without them?

The Circularity is Simultaneous (Dawr Ma'iyya)
Denying the viciousness of circularity by asserting simultaneity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a simultaneous circularity (dawr ma'iyya) in the motion of bodies is not logically problematic and how it answers the objection of infinite regress.

Core Idea
Not all circularity is vicious — simultaneous dependence avoids infinite regress.

This lesson presents a second answer to the objection that motion in a plenum leads to a vicious circle. The objection claimed that for one body to move into another's space, the second must move first, creating an infinite regress. The answer here is that the circularity is not vicious because the departure and arrival occur simultaneously, like the parts of a ring rotating together. This subtle distinction shows that not all logical circularity is invalid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the key phrase 'both occur together in terms of time' (يقع كلاهما معاً بحسب الزمان).
The passage is short but contains a complete argument.
2
Understand the comparison to a ring
Consider the example of a rotating ring: each part moves into the place of the adjacent part simultaneously. There is no need for a first mover in a chain; the motion is concurrent.
This analogy illustrates how simultaneous circularity works without logical absurdity.
3
Connect to the objection
Ask yourself: how does this answer refute the claim that motion in a plenum leads to an infinite regress? The answer denies that the circularity is vicious by showing it is simultaneous.
This is a key distinction in kalam: dawr ma'iyya vs. dawr tasalsuli (sequential circularity).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويمكن أيضاً الجواب عن هذا الإلزام بمنع بطلان الدور المذكور فيه فإنه دور معية توقف وتقدم فإنَّ انتقال الجسم عن المكان وانتقال الآخر إليه يقع كلاهما معاً بحسب الزمان كأجزاء الحلقة التي تدور على نفسها وليس يلزم من ذلك أن يكون دور
And it is also possible to answer this objection by denying the invalidity of the mentioned circularity in it, for it is a circularity of simultaneity, of dependence and priority. For the transition of a body from its place and the transition of another to it both occur together in terms of time, like the parts of a ring that rotates on itself, and it does not follow from that that it is a [vicious] circle.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage offers a second answer to the ilzam argument. The objection claimed that motion in a plenum leads to an infinite regress or a vicious circle (dawr) because for a body to move into another's place, that other must move first, and so on. The answer is that this circularity is not objectionable because it is a dawr ma'iyya – a simultaneous circularity where the two events (the departure of one body and the arrival of another) occur at the same time. The author compares it to the parts of a ring rotating together: each part moves into the place of the next simultaneously, so there is no vicious infinite regress or logical absurdity. The title of the lesson notes that this is a second answer distinct from the previous one about rarefaction and condensation.

Key Terms
دور
دور معية
إلزام
حركة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any logical circularity (dawr) is invalid and leads to absurdity.
Correction: The passage shows that simultaneous circularity (dawr ma'iyya) where events occur together in time is not invalid; it is like parts of a rotating ring.

Checkpoint
Why is the circularity in the motion of bodies not a vicious circle according to this answer?
A) Because the bodies are infinitely divisible.
B) Because the departure and arrival occur simultaneously.
C) Because God directly controls all motion.
D) Because the vacuum absorbs the motion.

Key Takeaway
A circularity where two events happen simultaneously is not a vicious circle and does not lead to infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you try to understand God's creation, do you always look for a 'first cause' in time, even when the Quran points to simultaneous, interdependent signs? Will you now learn to accept that some truths are co-instantiated without a temporal sequence?
?
The ring rotates without a single starting point—are you willing to trust that Allah's power sustains all things at once, without needing your mind to pin down a linear chain? What assumption about causality do you need to surrender today?
?
If you have ever dismissed a truth because it seemed circular, pause: Was your dismissal based on a rule that does not apply to Allah's timeless reality? Will you from now on distinguish between vicious circularity and the beautiful simultaneity of divine acts?

Rarefaction and Condensation Do Not Imply Vacuum
Objection and answer: rarefaction/condensation do not imply vacuum; matter changes magnitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the mutakallimun reject the claim that rarefaction and condensation necessitate the existence of vacuum, and how they explain volume changes through hyle's receptivity to magnitudes.

Core Idea
Volume change does not prove vacuum; matter itself is flexible in magnitude.

This passage presents a classic objection against the anti-vacuum position: rarefaction and condensation seem to require void between particles. The mutakallimun respond by denying this explanation and offering an alternative based on the nature of hyle (prime matter). Let's examine the objection and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the argument that rarefaction and condensation imply vacuum: when a body becomes larger, it must be due to more void between its parts; when smaller, less void.
The objector uses this to argue for the existence of vacuum.
2
Identify the mutakallimun's denial
Note the direct denial 'mamnu' and the alternative explanation: the change is not due to vacuum but to hyle's ability to receive different magnitudes.
The denial is immediate and firm.
3
Understand the concept of hyle
Hyle (prime matter) has no fixed magnitude in itself; it is equally receptive to small and large magnitudes, shedding one and putting on another.
This is the key to the refutation.
4
Reflect on the significance
This shows that observable physical change does not force acceptance of void; the inherent nature of matter suffices as an explanation.
The author notes this will be elaborated later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : التخلخل والتكاثف ( في الأجسام إنما يكونان ( لكثرة الخلاء وقلته ) فيما بين أجزاء الجسم فيكون مقداره مع كثرة الخلاء فيما بينهما كبيراً، ومع قلته صغيراً فهما يستلزمان وقوع الخلاء الذي هو المطلوب (قلنا: ممنوع) كونهما لما ذكرتم (بل) هما ( لأن الهيولى أمر قابل للمقدار الصغير والكبير إذ لا مقدار لها في حد نفسها ونسبتها إلى المقادير الصغيرة والكبيرة على سواء فتخلع مقداراً وتلبس مقداراً آخر أصغر أو أكبر ( وسيأتي ذلك فيما بعد
For it is said: Rarefaction and condensation in bodies occur only due to the abundance or scarcity of vacuum between the parts of the body, so its magnitude is large with abundance of vacuum between them, and small with its scarcity, so they necessitate the occurrence of vacuum, which is desired. We say: It is denied that they are as you mentioned; rather, they are because hyle is a thing receptive to small and large magnitude, since it has no magnitude in itself, and its relation to small and large magnitudes is equal, so it sheds a magnitude and puts on another magnitude smaller or larger. And that will come later.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair. The objector claims that rarefaction (takhalkhul) and condensation (takathuf) in bodies require vacuum (khala') between the particles: when rarefied, the object becomes larger due to more void; when condensed, smaller due to less void. The mutakallimun answer by denying this explanation. Instead, they attribute the change in size to hyle (hayula), the underlying substrate, which has no fixed magnitude in itself but dresses and sheds magnitudes as it receives them. The answer is a direct denial based on the nature of matter, and the author notes that this will be elaborated later. The core point is that apparent volume change does not prove the existence of actual void; it is a property of matter itself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That rarefaction and condensation necessarily imply actual void between particles.
Correction: The mutakallimun show that volume change is explained by hyle's receptivity to magnitudes without needing vacuum.

Checkpoint

B) The hyle's receptivity to different magnitudes
C) The body's own essential motion

Key Takeaway
Volume changes in bodies are explained by hyle's capacity to receive different magnitudes, not by the presence of void.

Answer that the circularity in motion is a simultaneity (dawr ma'iyyah)
This passage argues that the supposed circular motion is a simultaneous dependence, not a vicious circle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the concept of dawr ma'iyyah (circle of simultaneity) resolves the objection about circular motion in a plenum.

Core Idea
Not all circles are vicious: a simultaneous dependence avoids logical collapse.

This passage deals with a classic objection against motion in a plenum. The objector argues that any motion would involve a vicious circle. The text answers by distinguishing a circle of simultaneity from a circle of temporal priority, showing that the supposed circle is harmless.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse every word. Then read the literal translation to grasp the basic flow of ideas.
Pay attention to the parenthetical expressions; they clarify the context.
2
Understand the objection being answered
The objection claims that motion in a plenum leads to a vicious circle. Realise that the answer starts by denying the invalidity of that circle.
You do not need to know the full objection; the passage supplies enough context.
3
Identify the key term: dawr ma'iyyah
The term 'dawr ma'iyyah' is introduced as the type of circle. Understand that 'ma'iyyah' means simultaneity – both transfers happen together, not one after another.
Contrast this with a vicious circle where priority is impossible.
4
Grasp the ring analogy
The passage compares the motion to a rotating ring: as one part leaves a location, another part simultaneously fills it. Visualise how no temporal gap exists.
The analogy is key to seeing why the circle is not problematic.
5
See why this answers the objection
Conclude that the objector's inference that 'there is no circle' is false; there is a circle, but it is non-vicious. The answer defends the possibility of motion.
This lesson shows how kalam thinkers use conceptual distinctions to resolve logical puzzles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويمكن) أيضاً ( الجواب ) عن هذا الإلزام ) بمنع بطلان الدور المذكور فيه ( فإنه دور معية ) توقف وتقدم ( فإنَّ انتقال الجسم عن المكان وانتقال الآخر إليه يقع كلاهما معاً) بحسب الزمان ) كأجزاء الحلقة التي تدور على نفسها وليس يلزم من ذلك أن يكون لا دور
And it is also possible to answer this objection by denying the invalidity of the mentioned circle, for it is a circle of simultaneity, dependence and priority. For the transfer of the body from its place and the transfer of the other to it both occur together in time, like the parts of a ring that rotates upon itself, and it is not necessary from that that there be no circle.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage presents an answer to an objection about circular motion. The objector had argued that in a plenum, any motion would require a circle: the body leaves its place, another body fills it, and so on, leading to an infinite regress or a vicious circle. The answer denies that such a circle is invalid. It identifies the circle as a dawr ma'iyyah (circle of simultaneity), meaning the mutual dependence is simultaneous, not involving temporal priority. The example given is that of a rotating ring: as a ring turns, one part moves away and another comes to its place at the same instant. Therefore, no logical or temporal circularity is entailed. The conclusion is that the objector's inference that 'there is no circle' does not follow; rather, there is a circle, but it is non-vicious.

Key Terms

الدور
انتقال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That any circular dependence is necessarily vicious.
Correction: The passage shows that a circle of simultaneity (dawr ma'iyyah) is not vicious because the mutual dependence occurs at the same instant, avoiding the problem of priority.

Checkpoint

A) Vicious circle of priority
B) Circle of simultaneity (dawr ma'iyyah)
C) Circle of efficient causality
D) Circle of temporal precedence

Key Takeaway
The circle of simultaneity (dawr ma'iyyah) shows that mutual dependence can be non-vicious if it occurs at the same instant.

Two Meanings of Tawaqquf in the Circular Motion Argument
The author distinguishes two meanings of tawaqquf to refute the circular motion objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the critical distinction between two meanings of tawaqquf (dependence) and how this distinction refutes the objection of circular dependence in the motion argument.

Core Idea
Not all dependence implies priority—some dependence is mutual and harmless.

The objector claims that circular motion involves a vicious dependence between two transfers. But the term 'dependence' is ambiguous. This lesson unpacks the two meanings of tawaqquf and shows how the mutakallimun accept one meaning (mutual inseparability) while denying the other (priority-based dependence). By clarifying the terms, they dissolve the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the English translation. Note how the author presents two conditional statements: 'if he intends...'.
Pay attention to the phrase 'imtina' al-infikak' and 'bi-na't al-taqaddum'.
2
Distinguish the two meanings of tawaqquf
Identify the two meanings: (1) tawaqquf as mere inseparability, and (2) tawaqquf as inseparability with priority. Notice which one the author accepts and which he denies.
The first meaning can be mutual; the second implies one thing must precede another.
3
Understand why mutual inseparability is not vicious
Reflect on why mutual dependence (like parts of a rotating ring) is not a logical problem. The author accepts this possibility for the two transfers. This defuses the objection.
The key is that no temporal priority is involved.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة فإن أراد ) المستدل الملزم ) بالتوقف امتناع الانفكاك فقد يتعاكس ) التوقف بهذا المعنى فيكون من الجانبين ( وليس بمحال ) كما مر فيجوز أن يكون كل من انتقال الجسم مكانه وانتقال الآخر إليه متوقفاً على الآخر، أي يمتنع الانفكاك عنه ( وإن أراد ) بالتوقف امتناع الانفكاك بنعت التقدم منعناه (هاهنا أي منعنا أنَّ التوقف بهذا المعنى ثابت بين الانتقالين بل لا توقف بينهما أصلاً أو التوقف من جانب واحد فقط كما نبهنا عليه
In summary, if the objector intends by 'dependence' the impossibility of separation, then dependence in this sense can be mutual, so it is from both sides, and it is not impossible, as has passed. So it is permissible that each of the transfer of the body from its place and the transfer of the other to it is dependent on the other, i.e., inseparable from it. And if he intends by 'dependence' the impossibility of separation with the attribute of priority, we deny that here, i.e., we deny that dependence in this sense is established between the two transfers; rather, there is no dependence between them at all, or dependence from one side only, as we have indicated.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is a further clarification of the answer to the objection about circular motion. The author considers two possible meanings of tawaqquf (dependence). (1) If tawaqquf is defined simply as the impossibility of separation (without any temporal priority), then it can be mutual between the two transfers: each is inseparable from the other. Such mutual dependence is not impossible; it is like the parts of a rotating ring. (2) If tawaqquf is defined as the impossibility of separation together with the attribute of priority (one depends on the other in a prior way), then the author denies that such dependence exists between the two transfers. In fact, there may be no dependence at all, or only one-sided dependence. By forcing the objector to choose between these meanings, the mutakallimun defuse the charge of a vicious circle.

Key Terms
التوقف
امتناع الانفكاك
التقدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All forms of dependence entail a temporal priority (one thing must come before the other).
Correction: Dependence can simply mean inseparability without any priority, as in mutual dependence where each thing is inseparable from the other. This type of dependence is not a vicious circle.

Checkpoint
What is the second meaning of tawaqquf that the author denies in this passage?
A) Mutual inseparability without priority
B) Inseparability with priority (tawaqquf bi-na't al-taqaddum)
C) Absolute independence
D) Temporal succession

Key Takeaway
The circular motion objection is refuted by distinguishing tawaqquf as mutual inseparability (permissible) from tawaqquf with priority (not applicable here).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you argue about matters of faith, how often do you pause to define the key terms the way the author defines 'tawaqquf'? In what specific disagreement could you have avoided confusion by clarifying meanings first? Commit to doing that in your next discussion.
?
The objector assumed that dependence always implies priority. Where in your own life have you assumed that one thing must come before another—like repentance before forgiveness, or knowledge before action—when in reality they might be mutually dependent? How does this distinction challenge you to rethink your spiritual priorities?
?
Are you willing to accept that some truths can be mutually dependent without creating a logical contradiction? What comfort or discomfort does that bring you? How will you adjust your thinking to embrace this nuance?

Answer to the Objection of the Fish in Water
This passage answers an objection by analogy with a fish in water and invokes divine power to prevent water flow.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the mutakallimun defend against the objection that motion requires a vacuum, using the fish analogy and the concept of divine intervention.

Core Idea
Divine power can prevent natural consequences, so physical analogies do not force theological absurdities.

This passage addresses a sophisticated objection: if motion does not create a vacuum, then a fish swimming would force the entire sea to wave. The mutakallimun turn the objection around and then appeal to divine power to block the absurd consequence. The author also clarifies what their arguments actually prove.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the passage and identify the initial objection that would make a fish's motion impossible if there is no vacuum.
Notice the objector's assumption that water must fill empty spaces naturally.
2
See the response using divine power
The mutakallimun argue that the objector's reasoning leads to absurdities, but they reject the absurdity by affirming that God (the free agent) can prevent water flow.
This shows the role of divine attributes in kalam arguments.
3
Reflect on the meta-comment
The author points out that even if the arguments were sound, they only prove a place free of body, not the imaginary dimension (al-bu'd al-mawhum) that the mutakallimun hold.
This highlights a subtle distinction within their own school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد أجيب عن هذا الإلزام أيضاً بأنه لو صح لامتنع حركة السمكة في البحر إذ لا يعقل ثبوت خلاء في الماء لأنه سيال بالطبع يسيل إلى المواضع الخالية وإذ لا خلاء هناك فإذا تحركت سمكة في قعر البحر لزم تموجه بكليته لما ذكرتم بعينه فإن التزمتم هذا التزمنا تدافع أجسام العالم وتصادمها بحركة بقة واحدة وهو مردود إذ يجوز عندنا أن يمنع الفاعل المختار سيلان الماء إلى الأمكنة الخالية. واعلم أن ما تمسك به المتكلمون من الوجهين على تقدير صحته إنما يدل على ثبوت المكان الخالي. وأما كونه بعداً موهوماً كما هو مذهبهم فيحتاج إلى إبطال البعد المجرد الموجود
And this objection has also been answered that if it were valid, the motion of a fish in the sea would be impossible, since it is not conceivable that there be a vacuum in water, because it is fluid by nature, flowing into empty places. And if there is no vacuum there, then if a fish moves in the depths of the sea, it would necessitate the wave of the whole sea according to what you mentioned exactly. If you accept this, we accept the collision of the bodies of the world and their impact by the motion of a single gnat, and that is rejected because it is permissible for us that the free agent prevents the flow of water into empty places. And know that what the mutakallimun have adhered to of the two aspects, if supposed correct, only indicates the existence of a place free of body. As for it being an imaginary dimension as is their doctrine, that requires the refutation of the existing abstract dimension.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage provides a further answer to the objection that motion without vacuum leads to absurdities. The objector's reasoning is turned around: if his objection were valid, then a fish swimming in the sea would be impossible because the water would need to fill the space left behind, causing the entire sea to wave. The mutakallimun accept this consequence, but they then note that the same reasoning would force the objector to accept that the motion of a single gnat would collide the whole world—which is absurd. However, they reject this because they affirm the possibility of divine intervention: the 'free agent' (al-fāʿil al-mukhtār, i.e., God) can prevent the water from flowing into empty places. So the objection fails. Finally, the author adds a meta-comment: even if the mutakallimun's arguments were sound, they only prove the existence of a place free of body (makan khalin), not that this place is an imaginary dimension (bu'd mawhum) as their school holds. To prove that, one would need to refute the existence of an abstract dimension (al-bu'd al-mujarrad al-mawjud).

السيلان
البعد الموهوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mutakallimun's acceptance of the fish analogy implies they believe in an actual vacuum.
Correction: The passage shows that they accept the consequence only for the sake of argument, but ultimately they reject it by divine intervention, and they clarify that their arguments only prove a place free of body, not a vacuum as an imaginary dimension.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun defend against the vacuum objection by appealing to divine power to suspend natural laws, and they distinguish between proving a place free of body and proving an imaginary dimension.

The Two Senses of Tawqif in Reciprocal Motion
Al-Jurjani distinguishes two senses of tawqif to refute circular priority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani resolves the problem of circular priority by distinguishing two meanings of tawqif.

Core Idea
Not all mutual dependence implies a logical circle—only when priority is included.

This passage from al-Jurjani's Sharh al-Mawaqif tackles a classic objection: that two reciprocal motions must be mutually dependent, leading to an impossible circle. Al-Jurjani carefully dissects the meaning of 'dependence' (tawqif) to show that the objection fails unless a specific sense is intended. By distinguishing between mere impossibility of separation and impossibility of separation with priority, he refutes the claim of circularity.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then follow the English translation. Notice the key terms: التوقف, دور التقدم, امتناع الانفكاك.
The passage is dense; take your time to grasp the logical flow.
2
Identify the objection and the alternatives
The passage begins by stating an objection: each motion is a cause of the other, forcing circular priority. Then al-Jurjani offers three possibilities: neither is a cause, one is a cause, or both are causes. Focus on how he uses these to set up the discussion.
3
Distinguish the two meanings of tawqif
Al-Jurjani examines two meanings of 'tawqif' that the arguer might intend: (1) mere impossibility of separation, and (2) impossibility of separation with priority. Notice how each leads to a different conclusion about the motions.
This is the core of the lesson.
4
Understand the refutation
See how al-Jurjani denies the second meaning (with priority) for the two motions, while allowing the first meaning (without priority) to apply reciprocally. This refutes the objection without falling into circularity.
Reflect on why the distinction is important for kalam reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كل منهما علة للآخر حتى يلزم دور التقدم، بل يجوز أن لا يكون شيء منهما علة لصاحبه فلا يكون هناك تقدم أصلا، أو يكون أحدهما فقط علة للآخر فيكونان حينئذ كحركتي الإصبع والخاتم في أنَّ التقدم من أحد الجانبين ( وبالجملة فإن أراد ) المستدل الملزم ) بالتوقف امتناع الانفكاك فقد يتعاكس ) التوقف بهذا المعنى فيكون من الجانبين ( وليس بمحال ) كما مر فيجوز أن يكون كل من انتقال الجسم مكانه وانتقال الآخر إليه متوقفاً على الآخر، أي يمتنع الانفكاك عنه ( وإن أراد ) بالتوقف امتناع الانفكاك بنعت التقدم منعناه (هاهنا أي منعنا أنَّ التوقف بهذا المعنى ثابت بين الانتقالين بل لا توقف بينهما أصلاً أو التوقف من جانب واحد فقط كما نبهنا عليه
Each of them is a cause of the other so that circular priority is necessary; rather, it is possible that neither is a cause of its companion so there is no priority at all, or that only one of them is a cause of the other, and then they are like the motions of the finger and the ring in that priority is from one side. (And in summary, if) the obligating arguer (means by 'dependence' the impossibility of separation, then dependence in this sense may be reciprocal) and be from both sides, (and it is not impossible) as previously mentioned, so it is permissible that each of the transition of a body from its place and the transition of the other into it is dependent on the other, i.e., it is impossible to separate from it. (And if he means) by 'dependence' the impossibility of separation with the attribute of priority, we deny it (here, i.e., we deny that dependence in this sense holds between the two transitions; rather, there is no dependence between them at all, or dependence from one side only, as we have indicated).
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This is a logical analysis passage from al-Jurjani's Sharh al-Mawaqif. He is discussing an objection that two reciprocal motions (like two bodies exchanging places) must be mutually dependent, leading to circular priority. Al-Jurjani explains that such circular priority is not necessary. He first states the objection: each motion would be a cause of the other, forcing a circle. But he then offers possibilities: either neither is a cause, or one alone is a cause, like the motion of a finger and a ring. He then examines the meaning of 'tawqif' (dependence). If by 'tawqif' one means 'impossibility of separation' (which can be reciprocal), then it is not impossible for both motions to be interdependent. But if one means 'impossibility of separation with the attribute of priority', then al-Jurjani denies that such dependence exists between the two motions; at most, there is dependence from one side only. Thus the objection is refuted by distinguishing the two senses of tawqif.

Key Terms
التوقف (tawqif/dependence)
دور التقدم (circular priority)
امتناع الانفكاك (impossibility of separation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any mutual dependence between two events necessarily leads to a logical circle (دور).
Correction: The passage shows that mutual dependence is only circular if 'priority' is part of its meaning. If dependence means mere inseparability, it can be reciprocal without vicious circularity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the key distinction that refutes the objection of circular priority in two reciprocal motions?
A) The distinction between necessary and possible existence.
B) The distinction between tawqif meaning 'impossibility of separation' and tawqif meaning 'impossibility of separation with priority'.
C) The distinction between motion in a void and motion in a plenum.
D) The distinction between essential and accidental causes.

Key Takeaway
Al-Jurjani resolves the objection of circular priority by showing that tawqif as mere 'impossibility of separation' can be reciprocal without circularity, while tawqif including priority is not present in the motion exchange.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold two beliefs that seem to support each other, do you ever stop to ask whether one actually depends on the other for its truth, or if they merely coexist? Trace one such pair in your own mind. Can you identify which one, if any, has priority? What would it mean to you if you discovered a circular dependency in your own reasoning?
?
Think of a relationship in your life—with a friend, family member, or colleague—where you and the other person rely on each other. Do you view this mutual dependence as a healthy interdependence, or does it sometimes feel like an endless loop of obligation? How does the distinction between 'mere inseparability' and 'dependence with priority' help you understand that relationship better? What one step can you take today to move from a sense of circular burden to a clearer priority of care?
?
This lesson shows that not every reciprocal dependence is a logical flaw. Consider a deeply held conviction about your faith or worldview that seems to rely on other beliefs. Are you willing to examine whether that dependence is simply inseparable from your identity, or whether it actually conceals a hidden priority that might collapse if questioned? What would it cost you to honestly ask that question before God?

The Fish Objection and the Response of Free Choice
Al-Jurjani presents an objection regarding fish motion in water and answers it with divine free choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani defends the Ash'ari position on causality by invoking divine free choice to answer the fish motion objection.

Core Idea
Divine power can suspend natural laws, like water flowing to fill voids.

Al-Jurjani continues to address objections against the need for a void in motion. This time, he considers a scenario involving a fish swimming in the sea. The objector claims that such motion would require a void, which is impossible in water. Al-Jurjani's response reveals a key Ash'ari principle: God, as the free agent, can prevent the natural flow of water, thus allowing motion without a void. The lesson also clarifies the scope of the mutakallimun's arguments regarding empty space.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then the English translation. Let the argument's flow come through.
Notice the two parts: the objection and the answer, followed by a clarification.
2
Identify the objection
What is the objector's argument? Why would fish motion require a void? Understand the reasoning that if water flows to fill empty places, no void exists, so fish cannot move without causing global agitation.
The objector uses the same reasoning as before to create a contradiction.
3
Understand the response
Al-Jurjani answers that God, as the free agent, can prevent water from flowing into the space behind the fish. Thus motion is possible without a void. This shows divine power over natural laws.
This is a classic Ash'ari appeal to occasionalism.
4
Note the final clarification
Al-Jurjani adds that even if the mutakallimun's arguments were valid, they only prove the existence of empty space, not that it is an imaginary dimension. That requires further proof.
This differentiates two related concepts: empty place vs. imaginary dimension.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد أجيب عن هذا الإلزام أيضاً بأنه لو صح لامتنعت حركة السمكة في البحر إذ لا يعقل ثبوت خلاء في الماء لأنه سيال بالطبع يسيل إلى المواضع الخالية وإذ لا خلاء هناك فإذا تحركت سمكة في قعر البحر لزم تموجه بكليته لما ذكرتم بعينه فإن التزمتم هذا التزمنا تدافع أجسام العالم وتصادمها بحركة بقة واحدة وهو مردود إذ يجوز عندنا أن يمنع الفاعل المختار سيلان الماء إلى الأمكنة الخالية. واعلم أن ما تمسك به المتكلمون من الوجهين على تقدير صحته إنما يدل على ثبوت المكان الخالي. وأما كونه بعداً موهوماً كما هو مذهبهم فيحتاج إلى إبطال البعد المجرد الموجود
And this imputation was also answered by that if it were valid, then the motion of a fish in the sea would be impossible, because it is not conceivable that there is a void in water, since it is fluid by nature and flows into empty places. And since there is no void there, if a fish moves at the bottom of the sea, the sea would necessarily be entirely agitated by the very thing you mentioned. If you accept this, we accept the mutual pushing and collision of the bodies of the world by the motion of a single flea, and it is rejected because we hold that the free agent can prevent the flow of water into empty places. And know that what the mutakallimun clung to from the two arguments, if we grant their validity, only proves the existence of empty place. As for it being an imaginary dimension as is their doctrine, that requires invalidating the existing abstract dimension.

Commentary
This passage provides an additional answer to the previous imputation (about circular dependence). The objector claimed that if the objection were valid, it would make the motion of a fish in the sea impossible because water is fluid and would flow to fill any void, so the fish cannot move without creating a void. The objector argues that by the same reasoning, one would have to accept that the entire sea must be agitated, and even that a single flea's motion would cause all world bodies to collide. Al-Jurjani replies that this is not necessary because according to our (Ash'ari) school, the free agent (God) can prevent water from flowing into empty places, so a fish can move without causing global agitation. He then adds a note: the mutakallimun's arguments, even if valid, only prove the existence of empty space, not that it is an imaginary dimension as they claim; that requires further proof.

Key Terms
الفاعل المختار (free agent)
المكان الخالي (empty place)
بعد موهوم (imaginary dimension)
بعد مجرد (abstract dimension)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the fish objection definitively disproves the need for void, but al-Jurjani shows it can be answered by invoking divine power.
Correction: The objection is valid only if one assumes that natural laws are necessary and cannot be suspended. The Ash'ari response shows that divine power can override natural tendencies, so the objection does not disprove the possibility of void.

Checkpoint
How does al-Jurjani answer the objection that fish motion in water requires a void?
A) By claiming that water is not fluid.
B) By stating that the free agent (God) can prevent water from flowing into empty places.
C) By arguing that fish do not actually move.
D) By assuming that void exists naturally.

Key Takeaway
Al-Jurjani defends the Ash'ari view that divine power can suspend natural laws, so the fish motion objection does not disprove the possibility of void.

?
Do you truly believe that God can prevent water from flowing if He wills? If so, why do you often rely on natural causes as if they are independent agents? What would change in your daily reliance if you lived with this conviction?

Philosophers' First Proof Against Void
Al-Jurjani presents the philosophers' first reductio ad absurdum argument against the existence of void.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the philosophers' first proof against the void and how it uses a thought experiment with motion in different media to derive a contradiction.

Core Idea
The philosophers argue that void would make resistance irrelevant, contradicting our experience.

The philosophers attempt to prove that void cannot exist by showing that if it did, motion in void would be no faster than motion in a thin medium, which contradicts our intuition. They use a careful thought experiment with three motions. Let's read the proof and follow its logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the hypothetical nature: 'if void existed, let us suppose...'.
2
Identify the three motions
The proof considers three motions: one in void (1 hour), one in thick medium (10 hours), and one in thin medium (1 hour). Track each and note their times.
The second and third are 'like the first' in force, body, and distance.
3
Understand the key premise
The proof assumes that the time of motion depends only on the amount of resistance (consistency) of the medium. This leads to the calculation for the third motion.
The premise is that 'variation in time is only according to variation in resistance'.
4
See the contradiction
Since void has zero resistance, the motion in void should be faster than in thin medium, but the premises make them equal in time. This contradicts intuition, so void cannot exist.
The conclusion is 'this is a contradiction' (hadha khalaf).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج الحكماء) على امتناع المكان الخالي عن الشاغل سواء كان بعداً مفروضاً أو موجوداً ( بوجوه ) ثلاثة ( الأول أنه لو وجد الخلاء فلنفرض حركة ما ) إرادية أو قسرية أو طبيعية ( في مسافة خالية فهي في زمان لأن كل حركة إنما هي على مسافة منقسمة فقطع بعضها مقدم على قطع كلها فلا يتصور وقوعها في آن بل في زمان ( وليكن) ذلك الزمان ( ساعة و ) لنفرض حركة ( أخرى مثلها ) أي مساوية للأولى في القوة المحركة والجسم المتحرك ومقدار المسافة ) في ملء ) غليظ القوام كالماء ( فتكون هذه الحركة الثانية في زمان أكثر من زمان الحركة الأولى ( ضرورة وجود المعاوق ) الذي يقتضي بطء الحركة المستلزم لطول الزمان ( ولتكن) الحركة الثانية ( في عشر ساعات ( مثلا ) ونفرض ( حركة ثالثة ( مثلها ) أي مثل الأولى أيضاً في القوة المحركة والجسم المتحرك مقدار المسافة (في ملء آخر) رقيق كالهواء قوامه عشر قوام الملء (الأول) فتكون ( هذه الحركة الثالثة ( في ساعة أيضاً ) كالحركة الأولى ) لأن تفاوت الزمان ) في الحركات إنما هو ( بحسب تفاوت المعاوق ) فكلما كان المعاوق أكثر ، كانت الحركة أبطأ والزمان ،أطول وكلما كان أقل كانت الحركة أسرع والزمان أقصر (وهو) أي المعاوق (القوام) يعني قوام الجسم المالئ للمسافة الذي يخرقه المتحرك ) فإن كان المعاوق عشراً ) من معاوق آخر كالملء الثاني بالقياس إلى الملء الأول ( كان الزمان ( الواقع بإزاء المعاوق الأقل ( عشراً ) أيضاً من زمان المعاوق الأكثر ، كما في مثالنا هذا ( وإذا ثبت هذه المقدمات لزم أن تكون الحركة في الخلاء مع أنه لا معاوق ( عن الحركة في هذه المسافة ) والحركة في الملء الرقيق وهو معاوق ( عن الحركة فيه لاحتياج المتحرك إلى خرقه ودفعه ( كلاهما في ساعة ) كما ذكرناه ( فيكون وجود المعاوق وعدمه سواء ) حيث لم يتفاوت بهما حال الحركة في السرعة والبطء وإلا اختلف الزمان أيضاً ( هذا خلف ) لأن البديهة تشهد بأن الحركة مع المعاوقة وإن كانت قليلة تكون أبطأ أكثر زماناً من الحركة التي لا معاوقة معها أصلاً ) والجواب ) عن هذا الوجه كما ذكره أبو البركات ( أنه مبني على مقدمة واحدة وهي أن تفاوت زماني الحركتين الأخيرتين إنما هو (بحسب تفاوت وجود الخلاء إذ الأمور الأخر لا شك في إمكانها بل في وقوعها في الحركات أي الحركات المتحدة في المسافة والقوة المحركة ومقدار الجسم .
(The philosophers argued) for the impossibility of a place empty of an occupant, whether it be a hypothetical dimension or an existing one, (with three proofs) (The first: that if void existed, then let us suppose a motion) voluntary, compulsory, or natural (in an empty distance, then it would take time, because every motion is only over a divisible distance, and cutting some of it is prior to cutting all of it, so it cannot occur in an instant but rather in time. (And let) that time (be one hour, and) let us suppose (another motion) like it, i.e., equal to the first in motive power, moving body, and distance, (in a) thick (plenum) like water. (Then this second motion would take more time than the first motion) necessarily due to the existence of resistance, which necessitates slowness of motion entailing length of time. (And let) the second motion (be ten hours for example, and) let us suppose (a third motion) like it, i.e., like the first also in motive power, body, and distance, (in another plenum) thin like air, whose consistency is one-tenth of the consistency of the (first) plenum. Then (this third motion would also be in one hour) like the first motion, because variation in time in motions is only according to variation in resistance. So whenever resistance is greater, motion is slower and time longer; whenever it is less, motion is faster and time shorter. (And it, i.e., resistance, is consistency) meaning the consistency of the body filling the distance that the mover penetrates. (For if the resistance is one-tenth) of another resistance, like the second plenum relative to the first, then the time (corresponding to the lesser resistance would also be one-tenth) of the time of the greater resistance, as in our example. (And when these premises are established, it follows that the motion in void, with no resistance, (over this distance) and the motion in thin plenum, which is a resistance (to motion due to the need for the mover to penetrate and push it, (would both be in one hour) as mentioned. (Thus the existence of resistance and its absence are equal), since no difference in speed or slowness occurs between them; otherwise time would also differ. (This is a contradiction) because intuition testifies that motion with resistance, even if small, is slower and takes more time than motion with no resistance at all.) (And the answer) to this proof, as mentioned by Abu al-Barakat, (is that it is based on a single premise: that the difference in time of the last two motions is only according to the difference in existence of the void—since other factors are undoubtedly possible, even occurring, in motions, i.e., motions united in distance, motive power, and magnitude of body).
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' first proof against the possibility of void, followed by Abu al-Barakat's answer. The proof uses a thought experiment: suppose a motion in void would take some time (say one hour). Consider another motion in a thick medium (water) that takes ten hours due to resistance. Now consider a motion in a thin medium (air) whose resistance is one-tenth of water's; it should take one hour. But since void has zero resistance, the motion in void should also take one hour, making the presence or absence of resistance equivalent—contradicting our intuition that resistance always slows motion. Abu al-Barakat answers that the proof assumes only resistance (consistency) determines time, ignoring other possible factors like resistance from other sources (e.g., magnetic attraction) that could differ between void and plenum. Thus the reductio ad absurdum fails.

Key Terms
الخلاء (void)
المعاوق (resistance)
القوام (consistency)
الملء (plenum)
الحركة (motion)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers' proof shows that motion in void would be instantaneous.
Correction: The proof actually assumes motion in void takes time (one hour) and derives a contradiction not from infinite speed but from equality with motion in a thin medium.

Checkpoint
What does the philosophers' first proof conclude about the relationship between motion in void and motion in a thin medium?
A) Motion in void is faster than motion in thin medium.
B) Motion in void is slower than motion in thin medium.
C) Motion in void and motion in thin medium take the same time.
D) Motion in void is instantaneous.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' first proof against void uses a thought experiment with three motions to derive a contradiction from the assumption of void's existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof relies on logical reasoning from premises you may take for granted. Is there an area of your faith where you avoid such rigorous reasoning because it might unsettle you? Will you commit today to examining that area with the same honesty as these philosophers?
?
The philosophers were willing to entertain a false assumption (void) and follow it to a contradiction. What false assumption about yourself or your relationship with Allah have you been holding onto that you need to expose and abandon?
?
If even a small resistance (like air) would slow motion, but the void argument shows that no resistance and some resistance would be equal—this is a contradiction. In your spiritual life, have you ever treated a small sin as if it were no sin at all, while knowing it still creates distance from Allah? Will you confront that contradiction today?

Abu al-Barakat's Refutation of the First Proof
Abu al-Barakat critiques the philosophers' first proof by exposing its hidden assumption.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu al-Barakat's critique that the philosophers' first proof against void relies on the unwarranted assumption that only the medium's consistency affects motion time.

Core Idea
Abu al-Barakat shows the proof fails because other factors can affect motion time.

After presenting the philosophers' first proof, al-Jurjani gives Abu al-Barakat's answer. Abu al-Barakat points out that the proof assumes that the only variable affecting time is the resistance of the medium. He argues that other factors, such as magnetic forces, could also differ between void and plenum, breaking the reductio. Let's read his concise critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Abu al-Barakat's answer is short and to the point.
The answer begins with 'Wal-jawab'.
2
Identify the hidden assumption
Abu al-Barakat says the proof is 'based on a single premise' that time difference is only due to resistance. What is that premise exactly?
The premise is that 'variation in time is only according to variation in resistance'.
3
Understand the critique
He argues that other factors ('other things') are possible and even occur in motions, so the proportionality argument breaks down.
For example, magnetic attraction could affect motion differently in void vs plenum.
4
Reflect on the significance
This critique shows that the philosophers' reductio is not logically necessary; it relies on an unproven assumption. Therefore the existence of void remains a possibility.
This is a classic example of exposing a hidden premise in a philosophical argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج الحكماء) على امتناع المكان الخالي عن الشاغل سواء كان بعداً مفروضاً أو موجوداً ( بوجوه ) ثلاثة ( الأول أنه لو وجد الخلاء فلنفرض حركة ما ) إرادية أو قسرية أو طبيعية ( في مسافة خالية فهي في زمان لأن كل حركة إنما هي على مسافة منقسمة فقطع بعضها مقدم على قطع كلها فلا يتصور وقوعها في آن بل في زمان ( وليكن) ذلك الزمان ( ساعة و ) لنفرض حركة ( أخرى مثلها ) أي مساوية للأولى في القوة المحركة والجسم المتحرك ومقدار المسافة ) في ملء ) غليظ القوام كالماء ( فتكون هذه الحركة الثانية في زمان أكثر من زمان الحركة الأولى ( ضرورة وجود المعاوق ) الذي يقتضي بطء الحركة المستلزم لطول الزمان ( ولتكن) الحركة الثانية ( في عشر ساعات ( مثلا ) ونفرض ( حركة ثالثة ( مثلها ) أي مثل الأولى أيضاً في القوة المحركة والجسم المتحرك مقدار المسافة (في ملء آخر) رقيق كالهواء قوامه عشر قوام الملء (الأول) فتكون ( هذه الحركة الثالثة ( في ساعة أيضاً ) كالحركة الأولى ) لأن تفاوت الزمان ) في الحركات إنما هو ( بحسب تفاوت المعاوق ) فكلما كان المعاوق أكثر ، كانت الحركة أبطأ والزمان ،أطول وكلما كان أقل كانت الحركة أسرع والزمان أقصر (وهو) أي المعاوق (القوام) يعني قوام الجسم المالئ للمسافة الذي يخرقه المتحرك ) فإن كان المعاوق عشراً ) من معاوق آخر كالملء الثاني بالقياس إلى الملء الأول ( كان الزمان ( الواقع بإزاء المعاوق الأقل ( عشراً ) أيضاً من زمان المعاوق الأكثر ، كما في مثالنا هذا ( وإذا ثبت هذه المقدمات لزم أن تكون الحركة في الخلاء مع أنه لا معاوق ( عن الحركة في هذه المسافة ) والحركة في الملء الرقيق وهو معاوق ( عن الحركة فيه لاحتياج المتحرك إلى خرقه ودفعه ( كلاهما في ساعة ) كما ذكرناه ( فيكون وجود المعاوق وعدمه سواء ) حيث لم يتفاوت بهما حال الحركة في السرعة والبطء وإلا اختلف الزمان أيضاً ( هذا خلف ) لأن البديهة تشهد بأن الحركة مع المعاوقة وإن كانت قليلة تكون أبطأ أكثر زماناً من الحركة التي لا معاوقة معها أصلاً ) والجواب ) عن هذا الوجه كما ذكره أبو البركات ( أنه مبني على مقدمة واحدة وهي أن تفاوت زماني الحركتين الأخيرتين إنما هو (بحسب تفاوت وجود الخلاء إذ الأمور الأخر لا شك في إمكانها بل في وقوعها في الحركات أي الحركات المتحدة في المسافة والقوة المحركة ومقدار الجسم .
(The philosophers argued) for the impossibility of a place empty of an occupant, whether it be a hypothetical dimension or an existing one, (with three proofs) (The first: that if void existed, then let us suppose a motion) voluntary, compulsory, or natural (in an empty distance, then it would take time, because every motion is only over a divisible distance, and cutting some of it is prior to cutting all of it, so it cannot occur in an instant but rather in time. (And let) that time (be one hour, and) let us suppose (another motion) like it, i.e., equal to the first in motive power, moving body, and distance, (in a) thick (plenum) like water. (Then this second motion would take more time than the first motion) necessarily due to the existence of resistance, which necessitates slowness of motion entailing length of time. (And let) the second motion (be ten hours for example, and) let us suppose (a third motion) like it, i.e., like the first also in motive power, body, and distance, (in another plenum) thin like air, whose consistency is one-tenth of the consistency of the (first) plenum. Then (this third motion would also be in one hour) like the first motion, because variation in time in motions is only according to variation in resistance. So whenever resistance is greater, motion is slower and time longer; whenever it is less, motion is faster and time shorter. (And it, i.e., resistance, is consistency) meaning the consistency of the body filling the distance that the mover penetrates. (For if the resistance is one-tenth) of another resistance, like the second plenum relative to the first, then the time (corresponding to the lesser resistance would also be one-tenth) of the time of the greater resistance, as in our example. (And when these premises are established, it follows that the motion in void, with no resistance, (over this distance) and the motion in thin plenum, which is a resistance (to motion due to the need for the mover to penetrate and push it, (would both be in one hour) as mentioned. (Thus the existence of resistance and its absence are equal), since no difference in speed or slowness occurs between them; otherwise time would also differ. (This is a contradiction) because intuition testifies that motion with resistance, even if small, is slower and takes more time than motion with no resistance at all.) (And the answer) to this proof, as mentioned by Abu al-Barakat, (is that it is based on a single premise: that the difference in time of the last two motions is only according to the difference in existence of the void—since other factors are undoubtedly possible, even occurring, in motions, i.e., motions united in distance, motive power, and magnitude of body).
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' first proof against the possibility of void, followed by Abu al-Barakat's answer. The proof uses a thought experiment: suppose a motion in void would take some time (say one hour). Consider another motion in a thick medium (water) that takes ten hours due to resistance. Now consider a motion in a thin medium (air) whose resistance is one-tenth of water's; it should take one hour. But since void has zero resistance, the motion in void should also take one hour, making the presence or absence of resistance equivalent—contradicting our intuition that resistance always slows motion. Abu al-Barakat answers that the proof assumes only resistance (consistency) determines time, ignoring other possible factors like resistance from other sources (e.g., magnetic attraction) that could differ between void and plenum. Thus the reductio ad absurdum fails.

Key Terms
الخلاء (void)
المعاوق (resistance)
القوام (consistency)
الملء (plenum)
الحركة (motion)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers' proof against void is conclusive and cannot be refuted.
Correction: Abu al-Barakat shows that the proof relies on an unproven assumption that only resistance affects motion time, so it is not conclusive.

Checkpoint
What does Abu al-Barakat claim is the single premise on which the philosophers' first proof is built?
A) That motion in void is impossible.
B) That the difference in time between motions is only due to the difference in resistance.
C) That void has no resistance.
D) That all motions take time.

Key Takeaway
Abu al-Barakat refutes the philosophers' first proof by exposing its hidden assumption that only medium consistency affects motion time, showing the argument is not logically necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Abu al-Barakat exposed a hidden assumption that the philosophers were unaware of. What hidden assumptions might you be holding about your own ability to know God or about the nature of the world? Spend a few minutes this week listing one belief you hold and then honestly ask: 'Is this belief based on an absolute premise, or could other factors be at play that I haven't considered?'
?
The philosophers' proof seemed strong because it built on a seemingly obvious premise. In your own life, where have you accepted an argument or teaching without critically examining its underlying assumptions? Are you willing to be as sharp a critic of your own reasons as Abu al-Barakat was of the philosophers? Write down one such assumption and challenge it today.
?
Abu al-Barakat's refutation didn't require rejecting resistance entirely, only its exclusivity. Is there a truth in your faith that you treat as the only cause or explanation for something, ignoring other possible factors from the Divine? For instance, do you attribute spiritual growth solely to your own effort, or do you acknowledge Allah's unearned grace? Let this lesson remind you to widen your perspective and rely on God's complete control.

Baseline Time and Resistance in Motion
Al‑Jurjānī works through a numerical example to clarify the relation between time and resistance in motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al‑Jurjānī uses the example of motion in thick and thin media to distinguish baseline time (intrinsic to motion) from added time due to resistance, and why this prevents an erroneous conclusion.

Core Idea
Time for motion has a fixed baseline plus additional time from resistance.

This passage presents a concrete example to illustrate a key distinction: the time required for motion has a component that belongs to the motion itself, independent of any resisting medium. By computing times for thick and thin media, al‑Jurjānī shows that the time does not become zero when resistance is absent, thus avoiding a logical fallacy. The example is designed to refute an objection that would otherwise undermine the theory.

Guided Path
1
Read the example scenario
The passage describes motion in a thick medium vs. motion in a vacuum and in a thin medium. Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the numeric values.
2
Identify the baseline time
Note the claim: one hour is for the 'origin of motion' (aṣl al-ḥaraka) and has no connection to resistance. This is the intrinsic time of motion.
3
Understand the additional time from resistance
The thick medium adds nine hours due to resistance. The thin medium's resistance is a tenth of that, so its additional time is nine-tenths of an hour.
4
Compute the total time for thin medium
Add the baseline hour to the nine-tenths to get one and nine-tenths hours. This shows that time is not simply proportional to resistance.
5
Grasp the logical force of the example
The example prevents the objection that in the absence of resistance (vacuum) the time would be zero. It shows there is always a baseline time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ففي المثال المفروض وهو الحركة في الملء الغليظ تكون ساعة لأصل الحركة لا تعلق لها بالمعاوق أصلاً كما في الحركة الواقعة في الخلاء فإن ساعتها بإزاء الحركة دون المعاوق وتسع ساعات بإزاء المعاوق الذي هو الملء الغليظ فهذه التسع تتفاوت بحسب تفاوت المعاوق وتكون حصة القوام الرقيق من هذه التسع عشرا منها وهو عشر تسع ساعات وهي عشر تسع ساعات تسعة أعشار ساعة واحدة فيضاف تسعة الأعشار إلى ما تقتضيه الحركة لذاتها وهي ساعة فتكون حركته في الملء الرقيق في ساعة وتسعة أعشارها فلا يلزم المساواة بين وجود المعاوق وعدمه
In the supposed example, which is motion in thick fill, there is an hour for the origin of motion that has no connection to the resistance at all, as in motion occurring in a vacuum, for its hour is corresponding to the motion without the resistance, and nine hours correspond to the resistance which is the thick fill. So these nine vary according to the variation of the resistance. And the share of the thin substance of these nine is a tenth of them, which is a tenth of nine hours, and it is nine tenths of one hour. Then the nine tenths are added to what motion necessitates by itself, which is an hour, so its motion in the thin fill is in one hour and nine tenths of it, so equality between the existence of resistance and its non-existence does not follow.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage provides an example to illustrate the relationship between time and resistance in motion. The author sets up a scenario: motion in a thick medium (like thick air) takes a certain amount of time. He argues that there is a baseline time that belongs to the motion itself, independent of resistance, as seen in motion in a vacuum. The example posits that the motion in thick fill takes one hour for the ‘essence of motion’ (asl al-haraka) and nine additional hours due to the resistance of the thick medium. In a thin medium, the resistance is only a tenth of the thick medium's resistance, so the additional time is one tenth of nine hours, i.e., nine-tenths of an hour. This is added to the baseline hour, resulting in a total of one hour and nine-tenths. Thus, the time is not simply proportional to resistance; there is a fixed component, preventing the objection that time must be equal to zero when resistance is absent. The example is designed to show that the initial premise (that time difference equals resistance difference) only holds if motion itself does not necessitate a specific time.

Correction: Actually, motion has a baseline time independent of resistance, as shown by motion in a vacuum.

The Objection: Motion Cannot Necessitate a Definite Time
Later scholars present a reductio argument that motion cannot necessitate a definite time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that if motion necessitated a specific time, it would lead to an infinite regress of faster motions and thus a contradiction.

Core Idea
If motion required a specific time, that time would be infinitely divisible, generating a faster motion.

Later scholars challenged the view that motion inherently requires a specific time. They argued that if motion did require a definite time, that time would be the minimal possible, but since time is infinitely divisible, a motion in half that time would be faster, contradicting the initial claim. This lesson unpacks that objection as a reductio ad absurdum.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic passage from the beginning to 'ويتم الخلف'. This contains the full objection argument. Then read the translation to grasp the logical flow.
Notice the conditional structure: 'otherwise...'
2
Identify the assumption and its consequence
The argument assumes motion necessitates a specific time. That would make the motion the fastest, as no motion could occur in less time. Mark that step.
This is the key assumption that leads to the reductio.
3
Follow the divisibility argument
Time is infinitely divisible, so that supposed minimal time has a half. If motion occurred in that half, it would be faster. This creates a contradiction because we initially assumed the motion was the fastest.
The contradiction shows the assumption is false.
4
Understand the conclusion
The passage concludes that motion's essence does not necessitate a specific amount of time; rather, the time is determined by resistance.
This is the positive outcome of the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن المتأخرين من اشتغل ببيان أن الحركة لا تقتضي زماناً لذاتها وإلا لكانت الحركة الواقعة في ذلك الزمان أسرع الحركات إذ لا يمكن وقوع حركة في أقل من ذلك الزمان ولا يتصور كون تلك الحركة ولا كون حركة ما من الحركات أسرع الحركات لأنها واقعة في زمان والزمان منقسم إلى غير النهاية فيكون له أي لذلك الزمان الذي وقعت فيه تلك الحركة نصف ولو فرض وقوعها فيه أي في ذلك النصف كان الحركة الواقعة في النصف أسرع منها أي من الواقعة في الجميع بالضرورة إذا اتحدتا في المسافة فلا تكون تلك الحركة أسرع الحركات فظهر أن ماهية الحركة لا تقتضي مقداراً من الزمان بل الزمان كله بإزاء المعاوق فيتفاوت بتفاوته ويتم الخلف. وهذا الجواب الذي هو محصل ما ذكره الفاضل الطوسي إنما يتم لو بين أن وقوع الحركة في جزء من ذلك الزمان ممكن إما بحسب نفس الأمر وأنى له بيان إمكان وقوعها فيه إلا بحسب التوهم إذ يصح أن يتوهم وقوع الحركة في ذلك الجزء وأما بحسب نفس الأمر فكلا لجواز أن يقال: الزمان الذي تقتضيه الحركة قد لا يقبل القسمة بالفعل بل بالتوهم فكيف تقع الحركة المحققة في جزء وهمي
Among the later scholars is one who occupied himself with explaining that motion does not necessitate a time by itself; otherwise, the motion occurring in that time would be the fastest of motions, since it is not possible for a motion to occur in less than that time, and it is inconceivable that that motion or any motion is the fastest of motions, because it occurs in a time, and time is divisible infinitely, so that time in which the motion occurs has a half, and if its occurrence were supposed in it, i.e., in that half, then the motion occurring in the half would be faster than it, i.e., than the motion occurring in the whole, necessarily, if they are united in distance. So that motion is not the fastest of motions. Thus it appears that the essence of motion does not necessitate a specific amount of time; rather, all time is corresponding to the resistance, so it varies according to its variation, and the contradiction follows. And this answer, which is the gist of what al-Fadil al-Tusi mentioned, is only valid if he shows that the occurrence of motion in a part of that time is possible, either in respect of actual existence—and how can he show its possibility except according to imagination, for it is valid to imagine the occurrence of motion in that part? But in respect of actual existence, no, because it is possible to say: The time that motion necessitates may not accept division actually, but only imaginatively. So how can the actual motion occur in an imaginary part?
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from later scholars against the view that motion essentially necessitates a specific time. The argument proceeds: If motion itself required a definite time, then that time would be the minimum possible for any motion, making that motion the fastest. But time is infinitely divisible, so that time has a half; a motion occurring in that half would be faster, contradicting the claim. Therefore, motion does not necessitate a time; rather, all time is due to resistance. This leads to a conclusion that the earlier argument fails. The passage then presents the response of al-Fadil al-Tusi. His response attempts to show that it is possible for motion to occur in a part of that supposed time, but the commentator notes that this possibility is only according to imagination, not in actual reality. In actual reality, the time necessitated by motion may not be actually divisible, so the objection's reliance on actual division is not valid. Thus, al-Tusi's answer is only valid if actual division is possible, which is not proven. The passage thus questions the strength of al-Tusi's response.

A) Motion cannot exist in time
B) The supposed fastest motion would be slower than a motion in half its time

D) Time is not real

Key Takeaway
Motion's essence does not necessitate a specific amount of time; rather, time is determined by external factors like resistance.

Al-Tusi's Response and Its Critique
Al-Tusi attempts to defend the view by positing motion in a part of time, but the commentator questions its actual possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Tusi's attempt to defend the view by positing motion in a part of time, and the critique that this only works in imagination, not reality.

Core Idea
Al-Tusi's defense fails because it confuses imaginary divisibility with actual divisibility.

Al-Tusi responded to the objection by arguing that motion can occur in a part of the supposed minimal time, thus avoiding the contradiction. However, the commentator points out that this possibility is only in imagination, not in actual reality. This lesson examines the response and the critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the response passage
Read from 'وهذا الجواب' to the end. This contains al-Tusi's answer and the critique. Then read the translation carefully.
Al-Tusi's answer is presented as 'the gist of what al-Fadil al-Tusi mentioned'.
2
Understand al-Tusi's solution
Al-Tusi argues that it is possible for motion to occur in a part of that time, which would prevent the claim of a fastest motion. Note the phrase 'if he shows that the occurrence of motion in a part of that time is possible'.
This is the key move to escape the reductio.
3
Identify the critique
The commentator objects that this possibility is only valid according to imagination (توهم), not actual existence (نفس الأمر). In reality, the time necessitated by motion may not be actually divisible, so motion cannot occur in an imaginary part.
The critique hinges on the distinction between mental and actual division.
