(This is an elaboration of what preceded: that heat dissolves the dense and congealed, so it imparts to the body softness of consistency and sublimes the subtle and brings it out from among the parts of the dense, so the subtle joins its own kind and the parts of the dense also come together, thus condensation occurs from the perspective of position in each of them. And the pronoun was brought as masculine, either by interpretation of what was mentioned or because it refers to the masculine. i.e., for the hot thing to dissolve the dense by its heat.) (And it is answered that its action in water is transformation into air: for when heat affects water, some of it turns into air and moves by its nature upward. Then it mixes and parts of water adhere to that air and ascend with it, and that becomes vapor. So heat's action in water is transformation into air, (not separation) between its similar parts. (And) that its action (in egg is transformation in consistency, not (collection). For fire by its heat causes thickness in the consistency of the yolk and white. As for the union between them, it was already present before the effect of heat upon them. So the separation of the watery parts from each other did not arise from transformation but from adhesion, and it is not an act of heat at all. And by this the objection is repelled: if he meant that separation of similars is not an act of heat in the first place, then separation of dissimilars also likewise; if he meant that it is not an act of it at all, then it is prevented, because the separation occurring in similars did not occur except by means of heat and because of it. The commentator said: (By distillation) i.e., by distilling the watery parts from it.

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer concerning the effects of heat on similar and different substances. The objector argues that if fire supposedly separates different things and brings together similar ones, counterexamples exist: heat separates similar parts of water by evaporation, and brings together dissimilar parts like egg yolk and white. The answer distinguishes between direct and indirect effects. In water, heat transforms some parts into air, which carries water particles upward; the separation of water particles is not caused directly by heat but by adhesion to the rising air. In egg, heat thickens the consistency; the union of yolk and white was already present before heating. Thus heat's primary action is transformation, not separation or collection. The commentator further clarifies that the separation of similar parts occurs through heat's mediation, not as its direct act, resolving the objection.

Motion and Heat: Proof, Objection, and Answer
Al-Jurjani presents the proof, objection, and answer regarding motion generating heat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that motion generates heat, Abu al-Barakat's objection based on celestial spheres, and the two-part answer regarding the need for a receptive subject and the smoothness of surfaces.

Core Idea
Motion needs a receptive subject to produce heat; celestial matter lacks it.

In this passage, Al-Jurjani presents the fourth argument that motion generates heat, confirmed by experience. He then reports a powerful objection from Abu al-Barakat: if motion causes heat, the swift celestial spheres should be scorching hot, and the elements near them should ignite. The answer reveals a key principle of causal reasoning: an effect requires both an efficient cause and a receptive subject. We will trace this nuanced exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage has three distinct parts: proof, objection, answer.
2
Understand the empirical claim
The passage opens with the claim that motion generates heat, and that experience confirms it. This is the starting point of the argument.
Think about everyday examples like friction.
3
Analyze Abu al-Barakat's objection
Abu al-Barakat argues that if motion causes heat, then the celestial spheres (which move rapidly) should be extremely hot, and the nearby elements should ignite. This seems to contradict observation.
Consider why this objection is powerful: it uses the same principle to predict an absurd outcome.
4
Examine the two-part answer
The answer first states that the spheres' matter does not accept heat at all—heat requires a receptive subject. Second, the elements do not move with the spheres due to smooth surfaces, so they are not heated by motion. Then a refined answer: only fire follows the sphere, but its heat is resisted by the cold layer.
Note the logical structure: the answer does not deny the causal link between motion and heat, but adds the condition of a receptive subject.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رابعها أن الحركة تحدث الحرارة والتجربة تحققه. وقد أنكره أبو البركات وإليه الإشارة بقوله: إذا كانت الحركة تحدث الحرارة فيجب أن تسخن الأفلاك سخونة شديدة جداً بواسطة حركاتها السريعة، ويتسخن بمجاورتها العناصر الثلاثة التي هي في وسط الأثير، والأفلاك بمنزلة القطرة في البحر المحيط، فيصير هذه الثلاثة كلها بالتدريج ناراً لاستيلاء سخونة الأفلاك عليها مع مساعدة كرة الأثير إياها في تسخينها. والجواب أن مواد الأفلاك لا تقبل السخونة أصلاً، فلا بد في وجود الحرارة من المقتضي الذي هو الحركة ومن وجود القابل. وحينئذ فلا تسخن الأفلاك بسبب حركتها، فلا تسخن العناصر بالمجاورة. وليست العناصر متحركة على سبيل التبعية فإنها لملاسة سطوحها لا تتحرك بحركة الأفلاك فتسخن. ولهم كلام مناقض لهذا أن العناصر لا تتحرك بحركة الأفلاك، فسيأتيك في موقف الجواهر أنهم قالوا: النار تتحرك بتبعية الفلك، وليس التحريك يتعين أن يكون بالتشبث فيمنعها ملاسة السطوح، فإن الأفلاك عندهم يحرك بعضها بعضاً، ولا خشونة في سطوحها لتكون متشبثة بسببها. فالأولى في الجواب أن يقال: النار متحركة بمتابعة الفلك دون باقي العناصر، وليس سخونة النار توجب سخونة الباقي لأن برودة الطبقة الزمهريرية تقاومها.
The fourth [point] is that motion generates heat, and experience confirms it. But Abu al-Barakat denied it, and to him is the indication by his saying: If motion generates heat, then the spheres must become extremely hot by virtue of their rapid motions, and the three elements that are in the middle of the ether must become hot by their adjacency, and the spheres are like a drop in the encompassing sea, so all of these three would gradually become fire due to the heat of the spheres overcoming them, with the help of the sphere of ether in heating them. The answer: that the materials of the spheres do not accept heat at all, and for the existence of heat there must be the efficient cause, which is motion, and the presence of a recipient. Thus the spheres do not become hot due to their motion, so the elements do not become hot by adjacency. And the elements do not move in the way of following, because, due to the smoothness of their surfaces, they do not move with the motion of the spheres and so become hot. And they have a contradictory statement that the elements do not move with the motion of the spheres; it will come to you in the topic of substances that they said: Fire moves in following the sphere, and movement is not necessarily by clinging such that the smoothness of the surfaces would prevent it, for the spheres according to them move one another, and there is no roughness on their surfaces by which they would cling. So the better in the answer is to say: Fire moves in pursuit of the sphere without the rest of the elements, and the heat of fire does not necessitate the heat of the rest because the cold of the frozen layer resists it.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument concerning heat: motion produces heat, and this is empirically confirmed. Al-Jurjani then reports the objection of Abu al-Barakat: if motion causes heat, then the celestial spheres should be extremely hot due to their swift motions, and the three elements (probably fire, air, and water) would be heated by proximity, eventually turning them all into fire. The answer has two parts. First, the matter of the spheres does not have the capacity to receive heat, so even though motion is a necessary cause, the effect also requires a receptive subject, which the spheres lack. Hence they do not become hot. Second, the elements do not move as a consequence of the spheres' motion because their surfaces are smooth, preventing them from being dragged along. Then Al-Jurjani notes a potential contradiction: elsewhere it is stated that fire moves in pursuit of the sphere. The preferred resolution is that only fire follows the sphere, not the other elements, and the heat of fire does not infect the other elements because the cold of the frozen layer (al-zamharir) counteracts it.

الحرارة
التجربة
الأفلاك
السخونة

التبعية
الملاسة
الزمهرير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that motion always produces heat, regardless of the material.
Correction: The passage shows that heat generation also requires a receptive subject; the spheres' matter does not accept heat, so motion alone is insufficient.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do the spheres not become hot despite their rapid motion?
A) Their motion is not fast enough.
B) Their materials do not accept heat.
C) They are too far from the sun.
D) They are made of air.

Key Takeaway
Heat generation requires both motion and a receptive subject; celestial matter lacks the latter, so the spheres remain cool despite rapid motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that motion generates heat only when there is a receptive subject. In your own spiritual life, are there truths you have heard many times (like the importance of humility or gratitude) that have not 'heated' your heart? What might be missing—are you lacking the receptive quality that would allow those truths to affect you? Will you ask Allah to make your heart a receptive subject?
?
Abu al-Barakat assumed that because motion generates heat in common experience, it must do so in the celestial realm. Are there areas of your faith where you are overgeneralizing from your limited experience—assuming something must be true of the unseen world because it is true in the seen world? For example, do you think Allah's mercy or justice operates exactly like human mercy or justice? How can you guard against such overgeneralization?
?
The answer in this passage shows that the same cause (motion) can fail to produce its usual effect if the subject is not receptive. Think of a habit or sin you have struggled with. You may have tried many 'causes' to change—reading, advice, willpower—but if your heart was not truly receptive, change did not come. What would it mean for you to become a receptive subject for Allah's guidance? What barriers (like pride or laziness) must you remove?

Smooth Surfaces and Celestial Heat: An Objection and a Better Answer
Objection and answer regarding heat from sphere motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the smooth surface objection against celestial spheres causing heat and the better answer involving fire's motion and the frigid zone.

Core Idea
Smoothness breaks direct motion transfer, but fire still moves with the sphere, and cold opposes heat.

This passage from al-Mawaqif presents a classic puzzle: if spheres are smooth, how can they move the elements? And if they do, why don't they heat them? The text offers a refined answer that addresses these questions. Let's explore the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the claim that smooth surfaces prevent motion transfer.
The passage includes an apparent contradiction that is later resolved.
2
Understand the objection
The objection states that since the lunar sphere and fire's convex are smooth, sphere motion does not force element motion, so no heat.
This is based on lack of friction.
3
Identify the contradiction
The text notes that earlier they said fire moves with the sphere, but here they deny element motion. How can both be true?
The resolution is that only fire moves, and not through clinging.
4
Grasp the better answer
The better answer: fire moves in tandem with the sphere, but other elements do not. Furthermore, fire's heat is insufficient to warm the rest because the frigid zone's cold resists.
This preserves the observation that fire moves upward while explaining lack of heat.
5
Reflect on the implications
This shows how medieval thinkers refined causal explanations by distinguishing different types of motion and resistance.
It also demonstrates the use of smoothness to deny friction-based causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثالث - المقصد الأول : في الحرارة أنه جواب النفي، والحاصل أنَّ مقعر فلك القمر ومحدب النار سطحان أملسان، فلا يلزم من حركة أحدهما حركة الآخر فإذن أجرام الأفلاك ليست متسخنة بحركاتها ولا محركة للعناصر حتى يلزم سخونتها بوجه ما ( ولهم كلام مناقض لهذا الذي ذكروه ها هنا أن العناصر لا تتحرك بحركة الأفلاك ( فسيأتيك ( في موقف الجواهر ( أنهم قالوا : النار تتحرك بتبعية الفلك وليس التحريك يتعين أن يكون بالتشبث فيمنعها ملاسة السطوح ) فإنَّ الأفلاك عندهم يحرك بعضها بعضاً، ولا خشونة في سطوحها لتكون متشبثة بسببها فالأولى في الجواب أن يقال : النار متحركة بمتابعة الفلك دون باقي العناصر وليس سخونة النار توجب سخونة الباقي لأن برودة الطبقة الزمهريرية تقاومها .

قوله : ( على أنه جواب النفي أي لا حركة فلا تسخن. قوله: (وليس التحريك إلخ) هذا الكلام منع للسند فإن المجيب كان مانعاً للزوم حركة العناصر مستنداً بأنها ملساء فيجوز أن لا تتحرك بحركة الأفلاك. قوله : (فالأولى) قد عرفت وجه اختيار لفظ الأولى . قوله: (في الجواب أي عن شبهة لزوم حرارة العناصر بالحركة التبعية لا عن شبهة أبي البركات . قوله : (لأن البرد إلخ) متعلق بالنفي وعلة له . قوله : (النار تتحرك بتبعية الفلك قيل : الحق في هذه المسألة أنها تتحرك لكن لا بتبعية الفلك إذ حركته من نحو الشمال إلى نحو الجنوب، ولو كانت بالتبعية لكانت على موازاة العدل صرح به صاحب نهاية الإدراك فيه . قوله : ( فالأولى في الجواب أن يقال قوله في الجواب متعلق بحسب المعنى بأن يقال : أي الأولى أن يقال في أثناء الجواب : يعني بدل قوله : والعناصر لملاسة سطوحها إلخ وليس هذا جواباً عن تمام سؤال أبي البركات بل عن لزوم السخونة بحسب حركات العناصر.
The third observatory - first objective: On heat. It is an answer to the negation; the summary is that the concave of the lunar sphere and the convex of fire are two smooth surfaces, so motion of one does not necessitate motion of the other. Therefore, the bodies of the spheres are not heated by their motions nor do they move the elements so that their heat would be necessitated in any way. (And they have a contradictory statement here that the elements do not move with the motion of the spheres; it will come to you in the Station of Substances that they said: Fire moves by following the sphere, and moving is not necessarily by clinging; smoothness of surfaces prevents it.) For the spheres, according to them, move each other, and there is no roughness on their surfaces to cling by. So the better answer is to say: Fire moves in tandem with the sphere, not the rest of the elements, and the heat of fire does not necessitate the heat of the rest, because the cold of the frigid zone (al-ṭabaqa al-zamharīriyya) resists it.

His statement: 'on that it is an answer to the negation' meaning no motion so no heating. His statement: 'and moving is not [etc.]' this speech is a denial of the proof; for the respondent was denying the necessary consequence of the motion of the elements, relying on their smoothness, so it is permissible they do not move with the motion of the spheres. His statement: 'the better' you have known the reason for choosing the word 'better'. His statement: 'in the answer' meaning concerning the doubt of the necessity of heat of the elements by the motion of following, not concerning the doubt of Abū al-Barakāt. His statement: 'because the cold [etc.]' is related to the negation and a cause for it. His statement: 'fire moves by following the sphere' it is said: the truth in this matter is that it moves but not by following the sphere, for its motion is from north to south, and if it were by following it would be parallel to the equator, as stated by the author of Nihāyat al-Idrāk. His statement: 'the better in the answer is to say' his statement 'in the answer' is related in meaning: it is better to say in the course of the answer: meaning instead of his statement 'and the elements due to smoothness of their surfaces etc.' and this is not an answer to the entirety of Abū al-Barakāt's question, but about the necessity of heat according to the motions of the elements.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage presents an argument about whether celestial spheres cause heat in the elements through motion. The original text begins by stating that the concave surface of the lunar sphere and the convex surface of fire are both smooth, so motion of one does not force motion of the other. Hence spheres are not heated by their own motion nor do they move the elements to cause heat. However, the text notes a contradiction: earlier they said elements do not move with spheres, but later in the Station of Substances they said fire does move with the sphere. The explanation given is that sphere-to-sphere motion does not require roughness (they move each other despite smoothness). The better answer, the commentator says, is to assert that fire alone moves with the sphere, not the other elements, and fire's heat is insufficient to warm the rest due to the cold of the frigid zone. The commentator then explains each phrase: the initial 'answer to the negation' means no motion thus no heat; the denial of movement of elements is based on smoothness; and the 'better answer' specifically addresses the issue of heat from following motion, not the original doubt of Abū al-Barakāt. The commentator also notes that some authorities hold fire does move but not by following the sphere's motion because its direction differs.

الحرارة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if fire moves with the sphere, then all elements must move, or that smooth surfaces prevent any motion transfer whatsoever.
Correction: The passage shows that fire moves by following, not by being pushed, and that sphere-to-sphere motion does not require roughness. Smoothness only prevents forced motion, not following motion.

Checkpoint
According to the better answer, why does fire's motion not cause other elements to heat up?
A) Because fire is not hot enough.
B) Because the other elements do not move with the sphere.
C) Because the cold of the frigid zone resists the heat.
D) Because the spheres are too far away.

Key Takeaway
The smooth surface objection challenges heat causation, but the better answer restricts fire's motion to following and uses the frigid zone to explain why other elements remain cold.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there an area in your life where you have assumed a lack of direct influence means no influence at all? Where have you dismissed subtle, following forms of influence that God has placed in your path? Will you now look for them and respond?
?
When you encounter a strong counterargument to your faith, do you hastily conclude that the initial objection is final, or do you seek a deeper, refined understanding? Will you commit to not stopping at objections but pursuing the truth further?
?
What 'frigid zone'—what coldness or hardness in your heart—is resisting the warmth of divine guidance? Will you ask God to remove that barrier so that His light can reach every part of you?

The Objection that Fire Should Be Wetter Than Water
Al-Iji refutes the objection that fire is wetter than water based on the definition of wetness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Iji's refutation of the objection that fire should be wetter than water, and the distinction between thinness of consistency and the quality of wetness.

Core Idea
Wetness is not thinness; it is a distinct quality that facilitates shape acceptance.

Al-Iji presents a classic objection against the definition of wetness (al-rutuba) as a quality that facilitates acceptance of shapes. The objector argues that fire, being thinner, should be wetter than water. In this passage, Al-Iji not only refutes this but also clarifies the relationship between thinness, softness, fluidity, and wetness itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and identify the definition of wetness at stake
Notice that the objector uses the definition of wetness as 'ease of acceptance of shapes' and applies it to fire. The objector claims that because fire is thinner in consistency, it accepts shapes more easily and is therefore wetter.
The definition is implied in the objection.
2
Examine Al-Iji's first line of response
Al-Iji denies that pure simple fire is more accepting of shapes and that thinness alone is sufficient for ease of formation. He also notes that the fire we observe is mixed with air.
This refutes the direct inference.
3
Understand the second and deeper answer
Al-Iji explains that wetness is the quality that necessitates ease, not the ease itself. Moreover, a greater effect (ease) does not prove a greater cause (wetness) because the recipient may be more receptive.
This resolves the objection about air being wetter than water.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وربما لزموا أن النار يابسة عندكم وهذا التعريف الذي ذكرتموه للرطوبة يوجب كونها أرطب من الماء لأنها أرق قواماً من الماء والهواء أيضاً فتكون أسهل قبولاً للأشكال وتركها منهما والجواب منع ذلك في النار البسيطة أي لا نسلم أن النار الصرفة البسيطة أسهل قبولاً للأشكال من الماء وأن رقة القوام وحدها كافية في سهولة التشكل حتى يلزم أن يكون الأرق أسهل قبولاً وما عندنا من النار ليس بسيطاً بل هو مركب من الهواء و مختلط به فجاز أن يكون سهولة قبوله للأشكال وتركها بسبب اختلاط الهواء فلا يلزم قوله : ( لأنها أرق قواماً هذا التعليل يفيد بأن رقة القوام تقتضي سهولة قبول الأشكال وهو باطل وإلا لكانت الرطوبة عبارة عن رقة القوام نعم إنها تجامع رقة القوام واللين والسيلان وليست شيئاً منها . وبما ذكرنا ظهر الجواب عما ذكر من لزوم كون الهواء أرطب من الماء لأنه أرق قواماً منه قوله : ( وإنَّ رقة القوام وحدها إلخ يشعر بأنَّ رقة القوام لها مدخل أيضاً، وحينئذ يبطل تفسيرها بكيفية تقتضي سهولة قبول الأشكال. فالأولى أن يقول : وإن رقة القوام توجب سهولة التشكل. المحسوسات إنما هي البلة لا ما اعتبر فيه سهولة قبول أشكال حادثة لأن الهواء رطب بهذا المعنى ولا يحس فيه رطوبة. بقي هاهنا بحث وهو أن لزوم كون الهواء أرطب من الماء لم يندفع بشيء مما ذكرناه مع أنه باطل قطعاً، ويمكن أن يجاب عنه أيضاً : بأنَّ الرطوبة الكيفية المقتضية للسهولة المذكورة لا نفسها وكون الكيفية المذكورة في الهواء أزيد مما في الماء ممنوع، وزيادة الأثر لا تدل على زيادة المؤثر لجواز أن يكون بحسب القابل، وجرم الهواء لكونه أرق قواماً من الماء أقبل للسهولة المذكورة. وبهذا التحقيق يظهر اندفاع ما سيورده من لزوم كون النار أرطب من الماء والهواء لكونها أسهل قبولاً للأشكال منهما .
And perhaps they might adhere that fire is dry according to you, and this definition which you have mentioned for wetness necessitates that it is wetter than water because it is thinner in consistency than water and air, so it is more accepting of shapes and leaving them. The answer: deny that in simple fire, i.e., we do not accept that pure simple fire is more accepting of shapes than water, nor that thinness of consistency alone is sufficient for ease of formation such that the thinner is more accepting. And what we have of fire is not simple but is composed of air and mixed with it, so it is possible that its ease of accepting and leaving shapes is due to the mixture of air, so it does not follow. His saying: 'Because it is thinner in consistency' – this reasoning indicates that thinness of consistency requires ease of acceptance of shapes, which is false; otherwise wetness would be an expression of thinness of consistency. Indeed, it accompanies thinness of consistency, softness, and fluidity, and is not any of them. By what we have mentioned, the answer to what was mentioned about the necessity of air being wetter than water because it is thinner in consistency becomes clear. His saying: 'And that thinness of consistency alone etc.' suggests that thinness of consistency has an input as well; and in that case, its interpretation as a quality requiring ease of acceptance of shapes is invalid. So it is better to say: and that thinness of consistency necessitates ease of formation. Sensibles are only wetness (al-balla), not what is considered in it ease of accepting accidental shapes, because air is wet in this sense but no wetness is sensed in it. There remains a discussion here: that the necessity of air being wetter than water has not been repelled by anything we mentioned, though it is absolutely false. And it can also be answered that wetness is the quality necessitating the mentioned ease, not the ease itself, and that the mentioned quality in air is greater than in water is denied, and the increase of effect does not indicate the increase of the effective cause, because it may be according to the recipient, and the body of air, due to being thinner in consistency than water, is more receptive to the mentioned ease. By this investigation, the repulsion of what he will present about the necessity of fire being wetter than water and air due to its being more accepting of shapes becomes apparent.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the objection that fire should be wetter than water based on a definition of wetness (al-rutuba) as a quality that facilitates acceptance of shapes. The objector argues that since fire has thinner consistency (riqqat al-qiwām), it must accept shapes more easily and thus be wetter. The response has multiple layers: first, pure simple fire is not more accepting; second, the fire we see is mixed with air; third, thinness does not equal wetness—wetness is a distinct quality that accompanies thinness, softness, and fluidity but is not identical to them. A further answer adds that wetness is the quality necessitating ease, not the ease itself, and that a greater effect (ease) does not imply a greater cause (wetness) because the recipient (the body of air) may be more receptive. The passage systematically defends the definition of wetness and shows how air and fire can be wet without being sensed as wet.

Correction: Wetness is a distinct quality that facilitates shape acceptance; thinness accompanies it but does not define it.

Key Takeaway

Critique of the Response and Alternative Refutation
Al-Iji critiques the initial answer and presents two alternative refutations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Iji's critiques of the claim that fire's ease of shape acceptance is due to mixture with air, and the alternative refutation.

Core Idea
Fire's nature resists easy shape change, making mixture-based explanations problematic.

Al-Iji now turns to critique the earlier answer that fire's ease of shape acceptance is due to mixture with air. He presents two main objections: first, that this would imply fire is wetter than water, and second, that fire's nature alters what it mixes with, so mixing cannot preserve its form. He then offers an alternative refutation based on fire's inherent shape and the drying effect of earthy particles. This lesson walks through his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the overall flow.
Notice the structure: first a critique, then an alternative, then another critique.
2
Identify the first critique
Al-Iji objects to the claim that fire's ease of shape acceptance can be denied only in simple fire. He argues this would imply fire is wetter than water.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Examine the alternative refutation
Al-Iji suggests denying that fire accepts shapes easily at all, since it naturally takes only a conical form. But he notes that observation may show it conforms to a container.
This shows the complexity of empirical evidence.
4
Analyze the second critique
The deeper critique: fire alters what enters it, so mixing with air cannot preserve fire's specific form. Moreover, earthly particles in fire are dry and prevent shape acceptance.
Reflect on the role of dryness as a hindrance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والجواب منع ذلك في النار البسيطة) فيه بحث لأن هذا الجواب يشعر بأن تكون النار التي عندنا أرطب من الماء. وقد يجاب عن الأصل يمنع سهولة قبول الأشكال في النار مطلقاً فإنَّ النار لا تتشكل إلا على هيئة صنوبرية. ولا يسهل علينا أن نتخذ منها شكلاً مسدساً أو مثمناً أو غيرهما بخلاف الماء والهواء. فإنَّ اختلاف أشكال الإناء يستتبع اختلاف أشكالهما كما لا يخفى وفيه نظر لأنك إذا أوقدت ناراً وأطبقت من فوقها بإناء مسدس مثلاً فالظاهر أنَّ النار أيضاً تتشكل بذلك الشكل. قوله : ( بسبب اختلاط الهواء فيه بحث لأن النار في طبيعتها إحالة ما يداخلها وفي طبيعة الهواء قبول تلك الإحالة فكيف يتصور أن تداخل النار الهواء وتبقى على صورتها النوعية؟ فيفيد النار سهولة قبول الأشكال على أن مداخلة الأجزاء الأرضية للنار التي عندنا ربما يدعى أنها أكثر من مداخلة الهواء على تقدير ثبوتها كما هو الظاهر. فكيف لا تكون تلك المداخلة ليبوسة تلك الأجزاء المداخلة مانعة عن قبول الأشكال ؟ فليتأمل .
His saying: 'The answer is to deny that in simple fire' – there is discussion here, because this answer suggests that the fire we have is wetter than water. And it may be answered from the source that the ease of accepting shapes is denied in fire absolutely, for fire does not take shape except in a conical form. It is not easy for us to take from it a hexagonal or octagonal shape or others, unlike water and air. For the difference of the shapes of the container entails the difference of their shapes, as is not hidden. And there is consideration in this, because if you kindle a fire and cover it from above with a container that is hexagonal, for example, it is apparent that the fire also takes that shape. His saying: 'due to the mixture of air' – there is discussion, because fire in its nature alters whatever enters it, and air's nature is to accept that alteration, so how can it be imagined that fire intermingles with air and remains with its specific form? Then it would give fire ease of accepting shapes, while the intermingling of earthly parts in the fire we have is perhaps claimed to be more than the intermingling of air, if it is established, as is apparent. So why would not that intermingling, due to the dryness of those intermingling parts, prevent the acceptance of shapes? So reflect.

Commentary
This passage critiques the earlier answer that fire's ease of shape acceptance is due to mixture with air. It first objects that this would imply that the fire we have is wetter than water. Then it presents an alternative: deny that fire accepts shapes easily at all, since fire naturally takes only a conical shape in its own nature, though observation shows it may conform to a container. A deeper critique follows: fire alters what enters it, so it cannot mix with air without changing; moreover, earthly particles in fire would be dry and prevent acceptance of shapes. The passage ends by urging reflection, showing the complexity of the issue.

Wetness as Privation: An Infinite Regress Argument
Argues that if wetness is defined as receptivity of forms, it is privative, avoiding infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how defining wetness as receptivity of forms leads to the conclusion that it is a privative attribute, avoiding an infinite regress.

Core Idea
Wetness as receptivity is a privation, not a positive quality.

This lesson examines a logical argument from al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya regarding the nature of wetness. The author presents a dilemma: if wetness is interpreted as receptivity of forms, it is privative; otherwise, an infinite regress results. We will analyze this argument and its implications for understanding attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once carefully, then read the literal translation. Observe the logical structure of the argument.
The passage ends abruptly with 'وإن سلم'; this may continue in the original.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
Notice that the argument first considers interpreting wetness as receptivity of forms, then considers interpreting it as something else. Identify the consequence in each case.
3
Understand the infinite regress problem
Reflect on why needing another receptivity leads to an infinite regress, and why this is unacceptable in causal reasoning.
Infinite regress would require an endless chain of causes, which is considered impossible.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Recognize that the passage concludes that wetness cannot be a positive cause of receptivity, as the body itself is already receptive. This supports the view that wetness is a privative attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : ذكر في المباحث المشرقية أن الرطوبة إن فسرت بقابلية الأشكال كانت عدمية وإلا احتاجت إلى قابلية أخرى فيتسلسل، وإن فسرت بعلة القابلية فكذلك لأن الجسم لذاته قابل للأشكال. فلا تكون هذه القابلية معللة بعلة زائدة على ذات الجسم وإن سلم
The fifth [research]: It is mentioned in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya that wetness—if interpreted as the receptivity of forms—is privative; otherwise it would need another receptivity, leading to an infinite regress. And if interpreted as the cause of receptivity, likewise, because the body is receptive of forms by itself. So this receptivity cannot be caused by an additional cause beyond the body's essence, even if it is granted...
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage presents a logical argument from al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya concerning the ontological status of wetness. The argument is structured as a dilemma. If wetness is defined as the receptivity (قابلية) of forms, then it is a privative attribute (عدمية), meaning it is not a positive quality. If, on the other hand, it is defined as something else, then it would require another receptivity to receive forms, leading to an infinite regress (تسلسل). Similarly, if wetness is defined as the cause (علة) of receptivity, the same problem arises because a body is already receptive of forms by its own essence; thus no additional cause beyond the body is needed. The author concludes that this receptivity cannot be caused by an extra cause added to the body's essence. The sentence ends with 'وإن سلم' (even if it is granted), which suggests the argument may continue, but the passage as given stops here.

Key Terms
قابلية
عدمية
علة
تسلسل
صورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think wetness is always a positive quality.
Correction: This argument shows that wetness can be interpreted as a privation, i.e., the receptivity of forms, which is not a positive attribute.

Checkpoint
What problem arises if wetness is interpreted as something other than receptivity of forms?
A) It becomes a positive quality.
B) It leads to an infinite regress of receptivities.
C) It contradicts the definition of dryness.
D) It makes the body non-receptive.

Key Takeaway
Wetness, when understood as the receptivity of forms, is a privative attribute, not a positive quality, thus avoiding an infinite regress of causes.

Proof from a Ring Pulled by Two Equal Forces
Al-Jurjānī demonstrates a second proof for the principle of resistance using a ring and responds to objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Jurjānī's second proof for the principle of resistance (mudāfaʿa) using a ring held in equilibrium by two equal forces, together with the subsequent objections and clarifications in the glosses.

Core Idea
A ring pulled by equal forces reveals a resisting principle distinct from motion and power.

Al-Jurjānī presents a second, more refined proof for the existence of a principle of resistance (mudāfaʿa) that cannot be reduced to nature, force, or motion. Using the example of a ring pulled by two equal forces until it stops in the middle, he argues that each puller produces a distinct effect in the ring — an opposition (muʿāwiq) that is neither the resistance felt by the puller nor the puller's power itself. This effect, if unopposed, would draw the ring toward that direction and resist any impediment. From this, he infers a unique entity that necessitates pushing toward a specific direction, one that is not nature (since nature only moves up or down). The passage then engages with objections: some deny the absence of two resistances in the ring, some equate resistance with essential motion, and some treat the ring as a merely static object. The glosses, drawn from later commentaries, aim to clarify these points and defend the proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the main proof
Read the opening argument from the ring example carefully. Note how the existence of an 'opposition' (muʿāwiq) is argued to be distinct from resistance (mudāfaʿa) and from the puller's power.
The proof is dense; take time to parse each claim.
2
Examine the first clarification
The first gloss explains why the opposition in the ring is not resistance itself: because resistance is felt by the puller towards the opposite direction, not in the ring.
3
Consider the objection about two resistances
Some philosophers object that two resistances could coexist in the ring, just as a stone under water or a bladder held under water exhibits resistance. The gloss counters that the impossibility is only for two essential motions, not two resistances.
4
Analyze the alternative views
The passage concludes with differing opinions: one from Sharh al-Maqāsid stating each puller finds resistance to the opposite direction, and another claiming the ring is like a stationary object with no resistance at all.
The text is cut off here; note that the debate continues.
5
Reflect on the broader significance
Consider how this proof contributes to the larger discussion of causality: it attempts to establish a principle of resistance that is not reducible to the essential properties of bodies, thereby supporting the more nuanced causal framework of kalam.
This connects to the lesson's learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And one may argue to prove a principle of resistance (mabdaʾ al-mudāfaʿa) that the ring which two equal forces pull until it comes to rest in the middle—each of them has produced in it an opposition to what the pull of the other requires. And that opposition is not resistance itself (al-mudāfaʿa), for it is not present in that ring in this state at all, nor is it the power of the puller, for as long as he has not produced an effect in the pulled object, his mere power does not become an obstacle to the other's effect. So each of them has produced in it an effect other than resistance. And there is no doubt that what each has done is such that, if left without opposition, it would necessitate the ring's attraction to his direction and its resistance to what prevents it from moving in that direction. Thus it is established that there exists something that necessitates pushing towards a specific direction, and that is not nature itself because nature moves upwards or downwards. And what the two pullers have done... [Gloss:] His statement: 'and that opposition is not resistance itself' means the resistance of each to the ring towards his direction, because each one finds in himself resistance to the opposite direction. [Gloss:] His statement: 'and that opposition is not resistance itself etc.' because resistance to two directions is impossible by intuition. And one may deny the absence of two resistances in the ring in that state, for each of the two pullers finds in the mentioned ring what he finds in a stone suspended in air or in a bladder filled with air under water. The impossibility of the coexistence of two resistances to two directions is not conceded. What is impossible is the coexistence of two essential motions to two directions. It is said in Sharh al-Maqāsid: In a rope pulled by two equal forces to two opposite directions, each puller finds a resistance to the opposite direction. And it may be said: No, rather it is like a stationary object that prevents motion; there is no resistance in it at all. [Gloss:] His statement: 'and that is not nature itself' — it was said: it can be said that what you have mentioned is a requirement...
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage provides a second proof for the existence of a principle of resistance distinct from nature. It uses the example of a ring pulled by two equal forces until it stops in the middle. Each force produces an effect in the ring, and that effect is not the same as the resistance felt by the puller, nor is it just the force of the puller. The effect must be something that, if alone, would pull the ring towards that direction and resist anything preventing its motion. Therefore, there is a thing that necessitates pushing towards a specific direction. This thing is not nature because nature only causes motion up or down, not sideways. The marginal glosses clarify that the opposition in the ring is not the resistance felt by the puller, and that some deny the absence of two resistances in the ring, arguing that each puller feels resistance in the ring similar to the resistance felt in a stone held under water. They further argue that the impossibility is only of two essential motions, not two resistances. A reference to Sharh al-Maqāsid supports the view that each puller feels a resistance to the opposite direction. An alternative opinion says the ring is like a stationary object with no resistance at all. The last gloss is cut off but seems to challenge the claim that the resisting principle is not nature.

مبدأ المدافعة (mabdaʾ al-mudāfaʿa)
المدافعة (al-mudāfaʿa)
المعاوق (al-muʿāwiq) - the obstacle
الحركة الذاتية (al-ḥaraka al-dhātiyya) - essential motion

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The stationary state of the ring under equal forces implies there is no active principle of resistance; the ring is inert.
Correction: The proof demonstrates that each puller produces a distinct effect (muʿāwiq) in the ring, which, though not resistance itself, is a real entity that would cause motion and resistance if unopposed. The ring's equilibrium does not negate this active principle.

Checkpoint
What does the proof from the ring aim to establish?
A) That the ring is stationary due to equal forces.
B) That there is a principle of resistance (mabdaʾ al-mudāfaʿa) distinct from nature and force.
C) That two resistances can coexist in the same object.
D) That nature is the cause of all motion.

Key Takeaway
The ring proof demonstrates that a principle of resistance (mudāfaʿa) exists distinct from nature, force, and essential motion, as evidenced by the effect produced by each puller in a ring under equilibrium.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, when two opposing forces (e.g., desires, responsibilities) pull you to a standstill, do you assume nothing is happening inside you? This lesson shows that even in equilibrium, real effects are produced. What effects—good or bad—are being produced in your heart while you are torn between choices? Will you continue to ignore them, or will you examine them to understand where you are being pulled?
?
The ring's inner opposition is not the same as the resistance felt by the pullers. Are you more aware of the external pressures you face (like the pullers) than the internal state of your soul (like the ring)? Today, pause and ask: What 'opposition' is God producing in me through circumstances—an opposition that might be guiding me toward Him, even if I feel stuck?
?
The proof shows that a principle distinct from nature exists in the ring. Is it possible that your perceived 'stuckness' is not a failure but a sign that a deeper principle is at work? If you truly believed that God is the agent behind every effect, would you still resist His pull in your life? What would it take for you to stop fighting and let the opposition lead you to the middle—the place of submission?

The Argument for a Separate Principle of Resistance
Jarjani presents the argument for a separate principle of resistance (mudafa'a) and al-Razi's refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over whether resistance (mudafa'a) requires a distinct principle separate from nature and psychic power, and how al-Razi's objection is countered.

Core Idea
The ring pulled equally from both sides reveals that resistance is a distinct principle, not reducible to nature.

In this passage, Jarjani presents a key argument from the kalam tradition for a distinct principle of resistance (mudafa'a), which is said to govern the slowness of heavy and forced motions. Imam al-Razi objects that natural resistance is sufficient, but the example of a ring pulled by two equal forces is used to argue otherwise. By tracing the argument step by step, you will see how kalam thinkers defended the existence of a separate principle called i'timad.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its full literal translation without analyzing. Let the author's argument flow naturally.
Pay attention to the technical terms: i'timad, mayl, mudafa'a, tabi'a.
2
Identify al-Razi's objection
Al-Razi argues that nature itself is resistant to forced motion, so slowness does not require a distinct principle. Underline how he explains the slowness of larger bodies.
His key claim: 'tabi'a is resistant to forced motion.'
3
Examine the ring counter-argument
The passage then presents a hypothetical: a ring pulled by two equal forces, falling in the middle. Trace how each puller produces an effect that would move the ring toward him if unopposed.
Notice the logical steps: each puller acts as an obstacle, but that obstacle is not mudafa'a itself.
4
Understand the conclusion
The argument concludes that there must be a principle that requires pushing toward a specific direction, which cannot be nature (which moves up/down) or psychic power.
This establishes sensed mudafa'a as a distinct principle.
5
Reflect on the debate
Consider how this argument fits into the broader kalam framework of causal principles. Why was it important to posit distinct principles?
This connects to the atomistic occasionalism of kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثالث المقصد الثالث : الاعتماد وهو الميل البطء . وأجاب عنه الإمام الرازي: بأن الطبيعة معاوقة للحركة القسرية، ولا شك أن طبيعة الأكبر أقوى لأنها قوة سارية في الجسم منقسمة بانقسامه، فلذلك كانت حركته أبطأ فلم يلزم مما ذكر أن يكون للمدافعة مبدأ مغاير للطبيعة حتى يسمى بالميل والاعتماد وأما تسميتها بهما فبعيدة جداً ) وستقف في أثناء البحث ) عن أحوال الاعتماد ) على زيادات تفيدك ) زيادة اطلاع على هذا البحث وقد يحتج لإثبات مبدأ المدافعة بأنَّ الحلقة التي يجذبها جاذبان متساويان في القوة حتى وقعت في الوسط، قد فعل فيها كل واحد منهما معاوقاً لما يقتضيه جذب الآخر وليس ذلك المعاوق نفس المدافعة فإنها غير موجودة في تلك الحلقة في هذه الحالة أصلاً، وليس أيضاً قوة الجاذب فإنه ما لم يفعل في المجذوب فعلاً لم يصر مجرد قوته عائقاً لفعل الآخر فإذ قد فعل فيه كل منهما فعلاً غير المدافعة ولا شك أنَّ الذي فعله كل واحد منهما بحيث لو خلي عن المعارض لاقتضى انجذاب الحلقة إلى جهته ومدافعتها لما يمنعها عن الحركة في تلك الجهة فثبت وجود شيء يقتضي الدفع إلى جهة مخصوصة وليس ذلك نفس الطبيعة لأنها تحرك نحو العلو أو السفل، وما فعله الجاذبان بحسب ليس كذلك فظهر أنَّ للمدافعة المحسوسة مبدأ غير الطبيعة والقوة النفسانية.
The third observation, third aim: al-i'timad (inclination) is slowness of motion. And Imam al-Razi answered it: that nature is resistant to forced motion, and there is no doubt that the nature of the larger [body] is stronger because it is a power permeating the body, divisible by its division, so its motion was slower; therefore it does not follow from what was mentioned that mudafa'a (resistance) has a principle different from nature, such that it is called mayl (inclination) and i'timad (leaning). And as for calling them by these, it is very far [from correct]. And you will stand, during the investigation into the states of i'timad, upon additions that benefit you, increasing your knowledge of this research. And one may argue to establish a principle of mudafa'a that the ring which is pulled by two pullers equal in strength until it falls in the middle—each one of them has acted in it as an obstacle to what the pull of the other necessitates, and that obstacle is not the same mudafa'a, for it is non-existent in that ring in this state at all, nor is it the power of the puller, for as long as he has not acted in the pulled object with an act, his mere power does not become an obstacle to the act of the other. So since each one of them has acted in it with an act other than mudafa'a, and there is no doubt that what each one of them did, if left without opposition, would require the attraction of the ring to his direction and its mudafa'a against whatever prevents it from motion in that direction, so the existence of a thing that requires pushing towards a specific direction is established, and that is not the same nature because it moves towards up or down, and what the two pullers did accordingly is not so. So it became apparent that for the sensed mudafa'a there is a principle other than nature and psychic power.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage is an argumentative discussion on the nature of i'timad (inclination) and mudafa'a (resistance). It begins by defining i'timad as the slowness of motion. Then it records al-Razi's objection: slowness is due to the body's natural resistance to forced motion, and since larger bodies have stronger natures, there is no need to posit a separate principle called mayl or i'timad; calling nature by these names is far-fetched. The author notes that further investigation of i'timad will yield additional insights. Next, a counter-argument is presented using the example of a ring pulled by two equal forces. Each puller creates an obstacle to the other's pull. This obstacle is not itself the resistance (mudafa'a) nor the puller's power alone. Since each puller produces an effect that, if unopposed, would cause the ring to move toward him, there must be something that requires pushing toward a specific direction. This something cannot be nature, because nature moves toward up or down, not toward arbitrary directions. Therefore, a distinct principle of sensed mudafa'a is proven. The argument follows a sequence: definition, objection, counter-argument, and conclusion.

Misconception: Mudafa'a is a synonym for motion or a substance.
Correction: Mudafa'a is a distinct principle (mabda') different from nature and psychic power, as argued by the ring example.

Checkpoint

A) Because mudafa'a is not real
B) Because nature itself acts as a resistant force
C) Because the example of the ring is flawed
D) Because motion is identical to mudafa'a

Key Takeaway
The argument from the ring example demonstrates how kalām theologians used physical analogies to posit a distinct causal principle for resistance beyond natural and psychic forces.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In the ring analogy, two equal forces cancel each other out, yet each creates an internal obstacle. Is there a conflict in your life where two equal and opposite pulls—like worldly duty and spiritual devotion—produce a stillness that hides a deeper resistance? What does that resistance reveal about what you truly value, and will you follow it when the balance shifts?
?
The theologians argued that the principle of resistance cannot be reduced to nature because nature only moves up or down. When you feel pulled in a direction that is not your natural inclination—toward patience in anger, generosity in greed—where does that pull come from? If it is not from your nature, what does its existence demand of you today?
?
Al-Razi explained slowness as a function of natural strength, denying a separate principle. Do you ever explain away your spiritual struggles as 'just human nature'? Could it be that your very resistance to sin is a sign of a principle placed by the Creator, calling you to something beyond your nature? What would change if you saw every internal struggle as a witness to that principle?

Mudafa'a is Not Motion
Second point: mudafa'a is not motion, with two examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that mudafa'a (resistance) is distinct from motion, based on evidence from stationary objects.

Core Idea
Resistance exists even in rest, proving it is not motion.

The theologian presents a second point about i'timad: mudafa'a is not motion. The proof is simple: we observe resistance in objects that are at rest, such as a stone suspended in air or an inflated waterskin held underwater. This lesson unpacks that argument and its significance for kalam physics.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text and translation for the second point carefully.
2
Identify the claim
The passage claims that mudafa'a is not motion. What is the key evidence?
The evidence is that mudafa'a occurs during rest.
3
Analyze the two examples
Consider both examples: the stone in air and the waterskin in water. How does each show resistance without motion?
The stone resists falling downwards, the waterskin resists rising upwards.
4
Reflect on the significance
Why does this matter? It reinforces that i'timad is a distinct property from motion.
This distinction is foundational in kalam discussions of causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثانيها ) أي ثاني مباحث الاعتماد ( أن المدافعة غير الحركة لأنها توجد عند السكون فإنا نجد في الحجر المسكن في الهواء قسراً مدافعة نازلة و) نجد (في الزق المنفوخ فيه المسكن في الماء) أي تحته قسراً مدافعة صاعدة. ثالثها : له ) أي للاعتماد ( أنواع متعددة ( بحسب أنواع الحركة فقد يكون ) الاعتماد كالحركة إلى العلو والسفل وإلى سائر الجهات وهل أنواعه كلها (متضادة ) بعضها مع بعض ؟ اختلف فيه ( بناء على أنه هل يشترط بين الضدين غاية الخلاف والبعد أم لا ) يشترط فمن لم يشترط غاية الخلاف جعل كل نوعين من أنواع الاعتماد الجهات متضادين ومن اشترطها قال : إِنَّ كل نوعين بينهما غاية التباعد فهما متضادان كالميل الصاعد والهابط وما ليس كذلك فلا تضاد بينهما وإن كانا ممتنعي الاجتماع كالميل الصاعد والميل المقتضي للحركة يمنة أو يسرة ) فهو نزاع لفظي ) مبني على تفسير التضاد )
Second of them (i.e., second of the topics of i'timad): that mudafa'a (resistance) is not motion because it exists at rest; for we find in the stone forcibly held in the air a descending resistance, and we find in the inflated waterskin held in water (i.e., under it) forcibly an ascending resistance. Third of them: for it (i.e., for i'timad) there are multiple types according to the types of motion. So i'timad may be like motion to up and down and to other directions. And are all its types mutually contrary? There is disagreement about it, based on whether it is required between contraries the utmost opposition and distance or not required. So whoever does not require utmost opposition makes every two types of the types of i'timad [in different] directions mutually contrary, and whoever requires it says: every two types between which there is utmost distance are contraries, like ascending and descending inclination; and what is not so, there is no contrariety between them, even if they are mutually impossible to coexist, like ascending inclination and inclination that necessitates motion to the right or left. So it is a verbal dispute based on the interpretation of contrariety.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage presents two additional points about i'timad (inclination). The second point proves that mudafa'a (resistance) is not identical to motion. The proof is that mudafa'a exists even when a body is at rest, as in the case of a stone suspended in air that has a descending resistance, or an inflated waterskin held under water that has an ascending resistance. Since motion is absent, mudafa'a must be something other than motion. The third point concerns the types of i'timad. These are multiple, corresponding to directions of motion such as up, down, and lateral directions. The question is whether all these types are mutually contrary (mutadaada). The answer depends on how one defines contrariety. If one does not require the greatest possible opposition, then any two types from different directions (e.g., up and right) are contraries. If one requires the greatest opposition, then only types that are maximally distant, like ascending and descending inclination, are contraries; other pairs, though possibly impossible to coexist (like ascending and rightward inclination), are not true contraries. The author notes that this difference is merely verbal, based on the definition of 'contrariety'.

Key Terms
مدافعة
حركة
سكون
اعتماد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think resistance is simply a type of motion because it involves force.
Correction: The passage shows resistance exists even when the object is motionless, so it is a separate phenomenon.

Checkpoint
Why is mudafa'a considered distinct from motion?
A) Because it is found in living beings only
B) Because it exists even when the body is at rest
C) Because it is always upward
D) Because it is a type of force

Key Takeaway
Mudafa'a is not motion because it is present even when a body is stationary.

?

?
The stone in air resists falling, and the waterskin resists sinking. What is the 'direction' of your soul's resistance? Does it pull you toward this world or the next? What concrete step can you take to align your inner resistance with the path of Allah?

Types of I'timad and Contrariety
Third point: types of i'timad and the contrariety debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that i'timad has multiple types according to direction, and the disagreement about their contrariety is a verbal dispute based on the definition of 'contrariety'.

Core Idea
Whether two inclinations are contraries depends on the definition, not just incompatibility.

The third point about i'timad discusses its various types corresponding to directions of motion. The central question is whether all these types are mutually contrary (mutadaada). The author explains that the answer depends on a technical condition—whether 'utmost opposition' is required for contrariety. This leads to a debate that the author characterizes as merely verbal. This lesson unpacks that discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text and translation for the third point carefully.
2
List the types of i'timad
According to the passage, i'timad has types corresponding to directions of motion. What are the general directions mentioned?
Up, down, and other directions.
3
Understand the disagreement
The debate is about whether all types are contrary. What is the condition that splits opinions?
The condition is whether 'utmost opposition' (ghayat al-khilaf) is required.
4
Contrast the two views
Contrast the view of those who require utmost opposition with those who do not. Which pairs are considered contraries in each view?
Without condition: any two different directions; with condition: only maximally distant, like ascending vs descending.
5
Grasp the verbal dispute
Why does the author call it a verbal dispute? What does that imply?
The disagreement reduces to a difference in definition of 'contrariety'.

Second of them (i.e., second of the topics of i'timad): that mudafa'a (resistance) is not motion because it exists at rest; for we find in the stone forcibly held in the air a descending resistance, and we find in the inflated waterskin held in water (i.e., under it) forcibly an ascending resistance. So it is a verbal dispute based on the interpretation of contrariety.

Commentary
This passage presents two additional points about i'timad (inclination). The second point proves that mudafa'a (resistance) is not identical to motion. The proof is that mudafa'a exists even when a body is at rest, as in the case of a stone suspended in air that has a descending resistance, or an inflated waterskin held under water that has an ascending resistance. Since motion is absent, mudafa'a must be something other than motion. The third point concerns the types of i'timad. The author notes that this difference is merely verbal, based on the definition of 'contrariety'.

Repulsion is Not Motion
Iji proves that repulsion exists at rest, distinct from motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Iji proves that repulsion (al-mudāfaʿa) is a distinct phenomenon from motion, existing even at rest.

Core Idea
Repulsion exists even at rest—it is not the same as motion.

In this lesson, we examine Iji’s compact proof that repulsion is not motion. Using simple physical examples, he shows that repulsion can be present when an object is completely stationary. This distinction is crucial for the kalam theory of iʿtimād.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once. Then read the literal translation. Notice the concise style and the two examples.
The passage is only two lines but packs a full argument.
2
Identify the claim and the proof
The second topic of iʿtimād is that repulsion is not motion. The proof: it exists at rest. Find the two examples that illustrate this.
3
Analyze the examples
Consider a stone held stationary in air: it still feels a downward repulsion. Similarly, an inflated bag held underwater feels an upward repulsion. Both are at rest, yet repulsion is present.
This shows repulsion is independent of motion.
4
Reflect on the significance
Why does this matter? Because it distinguishes two fundamental concepts in physics and theology. Repulsion is a distinct property that does not reduce to motion.
This connects to the broader study of iʿtimād.

Source Text

Directions of the Celestial Sphere According to Specialists
Commentary explaining the specialist assignment of directions to the celestial sphere.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how scholars analogize the celestial sphere to a human body to assign directions, and the philosophical issue that arises when considering motion.

Core Idea
The directions of the celestial sphere are not fixed but depend on the type of motion and analogy to a human.

This lesson explores a technical discussion in Islamic cosmology: how specialists (al-khāṣṣa) assign directions like front, back, left, right to the celestial sphere. The passage uses an analogy to a man lying on his back and then critiques it by referencing Imam al-Razi's distinction between animals and non-animals. By the end, you'll see the complexity behind something as simple as 'which way is east?' in the heavens.

Guided Path
1
Understand the basic analogy
Read the passage where the sphere is compared to a man lying down with head to south. Identify which directions correspond to which parts of the sphere for eastern motion.
Notice that the assignment depends on the type of motion (eastern vs. western).
2
Examine the change for western motion
See how the directions except front and back change when considering western motion. Think about why front and back might stay the same.
This hints at a deeper principle about front/back being relative to motion.
3
Reflect on Imam al-Razi's distinction and the critique
Read the citation from al-Mabāḥith al-Sharqiyya about fixed front/back in animals vs. motion-dependent in non-animals. Consider why applying this to the sphere leads to a problem, and note the conclusion that front/back only make sense for the eastern half.
The commentary ends with 'محل تأمل' (a place for reflection), indicating the author's caution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ثم عمموا اعتبارها في سائر الأجسام قالوا : الفلك باعتبار الحركة المشرقية كرجل مستلق رأسه إلى الجنوب فيمينه المشرق ويساره المغرب وفوقه الجنوب وتحته الشمال وخلفه جهة سطحه الأعلى الذي سامت أقدام من في الربع المسكون وقدامه خلافه، وأما باعتبار الحركة الغربية فتتبدل جهاته إلا القدام والخلف. واعلم أنَّ الإمام ذكر في المباحث الشرقية: الن القدام والخلف حاصلان للحيوان حالتي الحركة والسكون، وأما غير الحيوان فانسلا بيعرضان له هاتان الجهتان عند الحركة فإن الجهة التي إليها الحركة يكون قداماً والتي عنها المتحركة يكون خلفاً، ومتى تغيرت الحركة تغير القُدام والخلف. ولا كذلك الحيوان فإنَّ قدامه وخلفه متحيتان بالطبع هذا كلامه فاعتبار قدام الفلك وخلفه على الوجه المذكور حينئذ محل تأمل، وإنما يظهر اعتبارهما عليه بالنسبة إلى النصف الشرقي.
His saying: 'Then they generalized their consideration to all bodies.' They said: The celestial sphere (al-falak), considering its eastern motion, is like a man lying on his back with his head to the south, so his right is the east, his left is the west, his above is the south, his below is the north, his back is the direction of its upper surface which is vertical to the feet of those in the inhabited quarter, and his front is opposite to that. As for considering its western motion, its directions change except front and back. And know that the Imam mentioned in al-Mabāḥith al-Sharqiyya: front and back are present in animals in both states of motion and rest. As for non-animals, these two directions occur to them during motion: the direction towards which the motion is is front, and the direction from which the mover moves is back. And when the motion changes, front and back change. Not so with animals, for their front and back are naturally fixed. This is his statement. So considering front and back of the celestial sphere in the mentioned manner is then a place for reflection, and their consideration appears only with respect to the eastern half.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage is a commentary that explains how specialists assign directions to the celestial sphere. They analogize the sphere to a man lying on his back with head to the south: right is east, left is west, above is south, below is north, back is the upper surface, front is opposite. For western motion, directions change except front/back. Then it cites Imam al-Razi who distinguishes between animals (fixed front/back) and non-animals (front/back determined by direction of motion). Applying this to the sphere, if the sphere's motion changes, its front/back would change, which is problematic. The commentary concludes that the consideration of front/back for the sphere is only apparent for the eastern half, implying a critique.

The Atomist Claim and the Waterskin Refutation
Al-Ījī presents and refutes the atomist explanation of heaviness and lightness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the atomist view that heaviness and lightness depend on the number of atoms and how a simple experiment with water and mercury refutes it.

Core Idea
Heaviness and lightness are not reducible to atomic count; equal volumes can have different weights.

In this passage, al-Ījī presents a core atomist thesis—that heaviness and lightness in bodies are due solely to the number of atoms—and then refutes it with a thought experiment involving a waterskin filled with water and then mercury. The experiment shows that equal volumes contain equal numbers of atoms, yet the weights differ drastically, disproving the atomist claim. The passage also introduces and dismisses the objection that water may contain void.

Guided Path
1
Read the atomist claim
Identify the statement that heaviness and lightness are due to fewness or abundance of atoms. Notice that the atomists deny that there is a separate accident called heaviness or lightness.
The claim is: 'Heaviness and lightness are due to fewness and abundance of atoms; there is no accident called heaviness or lightness.'
2
Analyze the refutation experiment
Consider the waterskin filled with water then with mercury. The container is the same, so the number of atoms (jawāhir farda) must be equal if atoms are indivisible and fill space. Yet mercury weighs many times more. This contradiction refutes the atomist view.
The refutation relies on the principle that equal volumes of different substances contain equal numbers of atoms if atoms are the fundamental building blocks.
3
Evaluate the void objection
The atomist might say water contains void (khalā’) that water does not fill, so water actually has fewer atoms. Understand why this objection is considered false.
The void hypothesis leads to absurdly large gaps between water particles, contradicting sensory experience of contiguity.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Note that the passage declares the void-based objection false because it would require the gaps between water particles to be enormous (e.g., twenty times the particle size), which is empirically false.
This strengthens the refutation: the atomist cannot escape by appealing to void.

Third observatory - third objective: On dependence, which is inclination. 5. The numbers of atoms, and lightness in bodies, are due to their fewness. So there is no accident in bodies called heaviness or lightness. And it is refuted by the fact that if a waterskin is filled with water, then the water is emptied and filled with mercury, then the weight of what fills it from mercury is many times greater than the weight of what fills it from water, while the parts (which are the individual atoms) in that mercury and water are equal, necessarily due to the equality of the container for both (i.e., for the mercury and water, which is the specific waterskin) so the parts filling it must be equal, unless it is said that in water there is void into which water does not flow naturally, either by the determiner or for another reason we do not know. Then its parts are not equal to the parts of mercury because they are many and contiguous, so there is no gap between them at all, or they are more compressed than the gaps of water. But this statement is false, as he indicated by his saying: (On that estimation, the increase (i.e., increase of void) over the parts of water should be parallel to the increase in weight of mercury over them, because heaviness and lightness are expressions of the two natural resistances. And it is not refuted by what is said: that the difference and variation may be by the act of the determiner, and that if it were valid, it would entail the impossibility of variation among them in other accidents like colors and tastes, and that it is permissible to attribute variation to the essences. As for the first two, it is obvious, for there is no argument about the permissibility of creating heaviness and lightness in them; the argument is about them being necessitated by their natures. As for the third, because individuation according to the theologians is privative, so external matters cannot be attributed to it.) His saying: (and lightness in bodies, etc.) i.e., the lightness of a body relative to another, like air relative to water, is due to the fewness of its parts, so it rises. So the inflated waterskin confined in water rises because of the fewness of the parts of air inflated in it relative to the water parts. And if that waterskin were filled with water, it would necessitate its floating on water. For homogeneity may be applied in the sense of similarity, but there is discussion in it. First, because what he mentioned does not conform to the principle of the theologians, which is that individual atoms are equal in accepting opposite attributes, and that difference is by accidents from the determiner. In short, the doctrine of the determiner and the comprehensiveness of His power repels the mentioned proof. Second, because it is permissible to attribute variation to essences. Third, because if it were valid, it would indicate the impossibility of variation in other accidents like colors and tastes and others. His saying: (and lightness in bodies is due to their fewness) If you say: if it were so, there would not be in the inflated waterskin placed under water an upward inclination, but the utmost would be that it does not find from the attractor above a descending resistance like what it finds in the one filled with water or mercury or the like, due to the abundance of void in it. I say: the existence of upward inclination in air is prevented, and the rising of the inflated waterskin is made due to the pressure of water on it, as will come, although there is in it what you will know. His saying: (so it would be necessary, etc.) contains discussion because it has been transmitted at the end of the discussions of quantity: that in the void, according to some, there is a force pushing upward, and perhaps the master [so it does not necessitate that on this estimation the increase of void over the parts of the filled be like the increase of the weight of mercury over them, so his saying 'so it would be necessary, etc.' is repelled]. And similarly the sensed lightness of air in the inflated waterskin, so ponder. Third observatory - third objective: On dependence, which is inclination. Like the increase of the weight of mercury over them (i.e., over the weight of the parts of water), for it is assumed that the increase of its weight is an expression of the increase of its parts, and there is no doubt that it is to the extent of the void in water. And it (i.e., the weight of mercury) is perhaps more than twenty (for example) times the weight of water. So for every part of water there are twenty parts of void. So the gaps between them (i.e., between the parts of water) are twenty times like the parts. And that is necessarily false, contradicted by the sense that witnesses to the contiguity between water parts.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the atomist view that heaviness and lightness in bodies are solely due to the number of atoms (جواهر فردة). The argument begins with the claim that there is no accident called heaviness or lightness in bodies; rather, these are reducible to the quantity of atoms: fewer atoms cause lightness, more cause heaviness. The refutation uses a bladder experiment: if a waterskin is filled with water, then emptied and filled with mercury, the mercury weighs many times more despite occupying the same volume, implying that if atoms were equal, the number of atoms in each case would be the same, so weight should be equal. This disproves the atomist claim. The objection is raised that water might contain void (خلاء) that the water does not fill, so the number of atoms in water is less. This is rejected because it would lead to absurd consequences: the gaps between water particles would be enormous (e.g., twenty times the size of the particles themselves), which contradicts sensory experience that water particles are contiguous. The commentary also addresses alternative objections: that difference could be caused by the act of the determiner (القادر المختار), or that it could be attributed to the essences (هويات). These are dismissed: the first is irrelevant because the discussion is about natural necessity, not God's power; the second is invalid because essences are privative and cannot ground real differences. Finally, there is a discussion about upward inclination in air: the author denies that air has an inherent upward inclination, attributing the rising of a filled waterskin to water pressure.

جوهر فرد
خلاء
اعتماد
ميل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Heaviness and lightness are entirely determined by the number of atoms in a body.
Correction: The waterskin experiment shows that equal numbers of atoms can yield vastly different weights, so heaviness and lightness must have another cause (e.g., a real accident created by God).

Checkpoint
What does the waterskin experiment demonstrate?
A) That mercury is heavier than water.
B) That equal volumes of water and mercury contain equal numbers of atoms but have different weights, refuting the atomist claim.
C) That void exists in water.
D) That heaviness and lightness are caused by the accident of weight.

Key Takeaway
The atomist explanation of heaviness and lightness fails because equal atomic counts yield different weights, disproving the claim that weight is due solely to atomic number.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted an explanation for something (like weight) simply because it seemed reasonable, without checking it against reality? What would it cost you to test your beliefs the way al-Ījī tests atomism?
?
If the waterskin experiment were presented to you as a layperson, would you have the intellectual humility to abandon a cherished theory? Or would you cling to it with ad hoc excuses like 'void'?
?
When you encounter evidence that contradicts your understanding of God's creation, do you respond by rethinking your assumptions or by inventing imaginary loopholes? Which response does this lesson demand from you?
